<div class="speech"><span class="ref">089</span> <div class="sentence">  i.</div><div class="sentence"> The first and simplest of all the principles of language, common also to the animals, is imitation.</div><div class="sentence"> The lion roars, the wolf howls in the solitude of the forest: they are answered by similar cries heard from a distance.</div><div class="sentence"> The bird, too, mimics the voice of man and makes answer to him.</div><div class="sentence"> Man tells to man the secret place in which he is hiding himself; he remembers and repeats the sound which he has heard.</div><div class="sentence"> The love of imitation becomes a passion and an instinct to him.</div><div class="sentence"> Primitive men learnt to speak from one another, like a child from its mother or nurse.</div><div class="sentence"> They learnt of course a rudimentary, half-articulate language, the cry or song or speech which was the expression of what we now call human thoughts and feelings.</div><div class="sentence"> We may still remark how much greater and more natural the exercise of the power is in the use of language than in any other process or action of the human mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">090</span> <div class="sentence">  ii.</div><div class="sentence"> Imitation provided the first material of language: but it was “without form and void.” During how many years or hundreds or thousands of years the imitative or half-articulate stage continued there is no possibility of determining.</div><div class="sentence"> But we may reasonably conjecture that there was a time when the vocal utterance of man was intermediate between what we now call language and the cry of a bird or animal.</div><div class="sentence"> Speech before language was a rudis indigestaque materies, not yet distributed into words and sentences, in which the cry of fear or joy mingled with more definite sounds recognized by custom as the expressions of things or events.</div><div class="sentence"> It was the principle of analogy which introduced into this “indigesta moles” order and measure.</div><div class="sentence"> It was Anaxagoras’ omou panta chremata, eita nous elthon diekosmese: the light of reason lighted up all things and at once began to arrange them.</div><div class="sentence"> In every sentence, in every word and every termination of a word, this power of forming relations to one another was contained.</div><div class="sentence"> There was a proportion of sound to sound, of meaning to meaning, of meaning to sound.</div><div class="sentence"> The cases and numbers of nouns, the persons, tenses, numbers of verbs, were generally on the same or nearly the same pattern and had the same meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> The sounds by which they were expressed were rough-hewn at first; after a while they grew more refined—the natural laws of euphony began to affect them.</div><div class="sentence"> The rules of syntax are likewise based upon analogy.</div><div class="sentence"> Time has an analogy with space, arithmetic with geometry.</div><div class="sentence"> Not only in musical notes, but in the quantity, quality, accent, rhythm of human speech, trivial or serious, there is a law of proportion.</div><div class="sentence"> As in things of beauty, as in all nature, in the composition as well as in the motion of all things, there is a similarity of relations by which they are held together.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">091</span> <div class="sentence">  It would be a mistake to suppose that the analogies of language are always uniform: there may be often a choice between several, and sometimes one and sometimes another will prevail.</div><div class="sentence"> In Greek there are three declensions of nouns; the forms of cases in one of them may intrude upon another.</div><div class="sentence"> Similarly verbs in -omega and -mu iota interchange forms of tenses, and the completed paradigm of the verb is often made up of both.</div><div class="sentence"> The same nouns may be partly declinable and partly indeclinable, and in some of their cases may have fallen out of use.</div><div class="sentence"> Here are rules with exceptions; they are not however really exceptions, but contain in themselves indications of other rules.</div><div class="sentence"> Many of these interruptions or variations of analogy occur in pronouns or in the verb of existence of which the forms were too common and therefore too deeply imbedded in language entirely to drop out.</div><div class="sentence"> The same verbs in the same meaning may sometimes take one case, sometimes another.</div><div class="sentence"> The participle may also have the character of an adjective, the adverb either of an adjective or of a preposition.</div><div class="sentence"> These exceptions are as regular as the rules, but the causes of them are seldom known to us.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">092</span> <div class="sentence">  Language, like the animal and vegetable worlds, is everywhere intersected by the lines of analogy.</div><div class="sentence"> Like number from which it seems to be derived, the principle of analogy opens the eyes of men to discern the similarities and differences of things, and their relations to one another.</div><div class="sentence"> At first these are such as lie on the surface only; after a time they are seen by men to reach farther down into the nature of things.</div><div class="sentence"> Gradually in language they arrange themselves into a sort of imperfect system; groups of personal and case endings are placed side by side.</div><div class="sentence"> The fertility of language produces many more than are wanted; and the superfluous ones are utilized by the assignment to them of new meanings.</div><div class="sentence"> The vacuity and the superfluity are thus partially compensated by each other.</div><div class="sentence"> It must be remembered that in all the languages which have a literature, certainly in Sanskrit, Greek, Latin, we are not at the beginning but almost at the end of the linguistic process; we have reached a time when the verb and the noun are nearly perfected, though in no language did they completely perfect themselves, because for some unknown reason the motive powers of languages seem to have ceased when they were on the eve of completion: they became fixed or crystallized in an imperfect form either from the influence of writing and literature, or because no further differentiation of them was required for the intelligibility of language.</div><div class="sentence"> So not without admixture and confusion and displacement and contamination of sounds and the meanings of words, a lower stage of language passes into a higher.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus far we can see and no further.</div><div class="sentence"> When we ask the reason why this principle of analogy prevails in all the vast domain of language, there is no answer to the question; or no other answer but this, that there are innumerable ways in which, like number, analogy permeates, not only language, but the whole world, both visible and intellectual.</div><div class="sentence"> We know from experience that it does not (a) arise from any conscious act of reflection that the accusative of a Latin noun in “us” should end in “um;” nor (b) from any necessity of being understood,—much less articulation would suffice for this; nor (c) from greater convenience or expressiveness of particular sounds.</div><div class="sentence"> Such notions were certainly far enough away from the mind of primitive man.</div><div class="sentence"> We may speak of a latent instinct, of a survival of the fittest, easiest, most euphonic, most economical of breath, in the case of one of two competing sounds; but these expressions do not add anything to our knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> We may try to grasp the infinity of language either under the figure of a limitless plain divided into countries and districts by natural boundaries, or of a vast river eternally flowing whose origin is concealed from us; we may apprehend partially the laws by which speech is regulated: but we do not know, and we seem as if we should never know, any more than in the parallel case of the origin of species, how vocal sounds received life and grew, and in the form of languages came to be distributed over the earth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">093</span> <div class="sentence">  iii.</div><div class="sentence"> Next in order to analogy in the formation of language or even prior to it comes the principle of onomatopea, which is itself a kind of analogy or similarity of sound and meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> In by far the greater number of words it has become disguised and has disappeared; but in no stage of language is it entirely lost.</div><div class="sentence"> It belongs chiefly to early language, in which words were few; and its influence grew less and less as time went on.</div><div class="sentence"> To the ear which had a sense of harmony it became a barbarism which disturbed the flow and equilibrium of discourse; it was an excrescence which had to be cut out, a survival which needed to be got rid of, because it was out of keeping with the rest.</div><div class="sentence"> It remained for the most part only as a formative principle, which used words and letters not as crude imitations of other natural sounds, but as symbols of ideas which were naturally associated with them.</div><div class="sentence"> It received in another way a new character; it affected not so much single words, as larger portions of human speech.</div><div class="sentence"> It regulated the juxtaposition of sounds and the cadence of sentences.</div><div class="sentence"> It was the music, not of song, but of speech, in prose as well as verse.</div><div class="sentence"> The old onomatopea of primitive language was refined into an onomatopea of a higher kind, in which it is no longer true to say that a particular sound corresponds to a motion or action of man or beast or movement of nature, but that in all the higher uses of language the sound is the echo of the sense, especially in poetry, in which beauty and expressiveness are given to human thoughts by the harmonious composition of the words, syllables, letters, accents, quantities, rhythms, rhymes, varieties and contrasts of all sorts.</div><div class="sentence"> The poet with his “Break, break, break” or his e pasin nekuessi kataphthimenoisin anassein or his “longius ex altoque sinum trahit,” can produce a far finer music than any crude imitations of things or actions in sound, although a letter or two having this imitative power may be a lesser element of beauty in such passages.</div><div class="sentence"> The same subtle sensibility, which adapts the word to the thing, adapts the sentence or cadence to the general meaning or spirit of the passage.</div><div class="sentence"> This is the higher onomatopea which has banished the cruder sort as unworthy to have a place in great languages and literatures.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">094</span> <div class="sentence">  We can see clearly enough that letters or collocations of letters do by various degrees of strength or weakness, length or shortness, emphasis or pitch, become the natural expressions of the finer parts of human feeling or thought.</div><div class="sentence"> And not only so, but letters themselves have a significance; as Plato observes that the letter rho accent is expressive of motion, the letters delta and tau of binding and rest, the letter lambda of smoothness, nu of inwardness, the letter eta of length, the letter omicron of roundness.</div><div class="sentence"> These were often combined so as to form composite notions, as for example in tromos (trembling), trachus (rugged), thrauein (crush), krouein (strike), thruptein (break), pumbein (whirl),—in all which words we notice a parallel composition of sounds in their English equivalents.</div><div class="sentence"> Plato also remarks, as we remark, that the onomatopoetic principle is far from prevailing uniformly, and further that no explanation of language consistently corresponds with any system of philosophy, however great may be the light which language throws upon the nature of the mind.</div><div class="sentence"> Both in Greek and English we find groups of words such as string, swing, sling, spring, sting, which are parallel to one another and may be said to derive their vocal effect partly from contrast of letters, but in which it is impossible to assign a precise amount of meaning to each of the expressive and onomatopoetic letters.</div><div class="sentence"> A few of them are directly imitative, as for example the omega in oon, which represents the round form of the egg by the figure of the mouth: or bronte (thunder), in which the fulness of the sound of the word corresponds to the thing signified by it; or bombos (buzzing), of which the first syllable, as in its English equivalent, has the meaning of a deep sound.</div><div class="sentence"> We may observe also (as we see in the case of the poor stammerer) that speech has the co-operation of the whole body and may be often assisted or half expressed by gesticulation.</div><div class="sentence"> A sound or word is not the work of the vocal organs only; nearly the whole of the upper part of the human frame, including head, chest, lungs, have a share in creating it; and it may be accompanied by a movement of the eyes, nose, fingers, hands, feet which contributes to the effect of it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">095</span> <div class="sentence">  The principle of onomatopea has fallen into discredit, partly because it has been supposed to imply an actual manufacture of words out of syllables and letters, like a piece of joiner’s work,—a theory of language which is more and more refuted by facts, and more and more going out of fashion with philologians; and partly also because the traces of onomatopea in separate words become almost obliterated in the course of ages.</div><div class="sentence"> The poet of language cannot put in and pull out letters, as a painter might insert or blot out a shade of colour to give effect to his picture.</div><div class="sentence"> It would be ridiculous for him to alter any received form of a word in order to render it more expressive of the sense.</div><div class="sentence"> He can only select, perhaps out of some dialect, the form which is already best adapted to his purpose.</div><div class="sentence"> The true onomatopea is not a creative, but a formative principle, which in the later stage of the history of language ceases to act upon individual words; but still works through the collocation of them in the sentence or paragraph, and the adaptation of every word, syllable, letter to one another and to the rhythm of the whole passage.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">096</span> <div class="sentence">  iv.</div><div class="sentence"> Next, under a distinct head, although not separable from the preceding, may be considered the differentiation of languages, i.e.</div><div class="sentence"> the manner in which differences of meaning and form have arisen in them.</div><div class="sentence"> Into their first creation we have ceased to enquire: it is their aftergrowth with which we are now concerned.</div><div class="sentence"> How did the roots or substantial portions of words become modified or inflected?</div><div class="sentence"> and how did they receive separate meanings?</div><div class="sentence"> First we remark that words are attracted by the sounds and senses of other words, so that they form groups of nouns and verbs analogous in sound and sense to one another, each noun or verb putting forth inflexions, generally of two or three patterns, and with exceptions.</div><div class="sentence"> We do not say that we know how sense became first allied to sound; but we have no difficulty in ascertaining how the sounds and meanings of words were in time parted off or differentiated.</div><div class="sentence"> (1) The chief causes which regulate the variations of sound are (a) double or differing analogies, which lead sometimes to one form, sometimes to another (b) euphony, by which is meant chiefly the greater pleasure to the ear and the greater facility to the organs of speech which is given by a new formation or pronunciation of a word (c) the necessity of finding new expressions for new classes or processes of things.</div><div class="sentence"> We are told that changes of sound take place by innumerable gradations until a whole tribe or community or society find themselves acquiescing in a new pronunciation or use of language.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet no one observes the change, or is at all aware that in the course of a lifetime he and his contemporaries have appreciably varied their intonation or use of words.</div><div class="sentence"> On the other hand, the necessities of language seem to require that the intermediate sounds or meanings of words should quickly become fixed or set and not continue in a state of transition.</div><div class="sentence"> The process of settling down is aided by the organs of speech and by the use of writing and printing.</div><div class="sentence"> (2) The meaning of words varies because ideas vary or the number of things which is included under them or with which they are associated is increased.</div><div class="sentence"> A single word is thus made to do duty for many more things than were formerly expressed by it; and it parts into different senses when the classes of things or ideas which are represented by it are themselves different and distinct.</div><div class="sentence"> A figurative use of a word may easily pass into a new sense: a new meaning caught up by association may become more important than all the rest.</div><div class="sentence"> The good or neutral sense of a word, such as Jesuit, Puritan, Methodist, Heretic, has been often converted into a bad one by the malevolence of party spirit.</div><div class="sentence"> Double forms suggest different meanings and are often used to express them; and the form or accent of a word has been not unfrequently altered when there is a difference of meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> The difference of gender in nouns is utilized for the same reason.</div><div class="sentence"> New meanings of words push themselves into the vacant spaces of language and retire when they are no longer needed.</div><div class="sentence"> Language equally abhors vacancy and superfluity.</div><div class="sentence"> But the remedial measures by which both are eliminated are not due to any conscious action of the human mind; nor is the force exerted by them constraining or necessary.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">097</span> <div class="sentence">  (7) We have shown that language, although subject to laws, is far from being of an exact and uniform nature.</div><div class="sentence"> We may now speak briefly of the faults of language.</div><div class="sentence"> They may be compared to the faults of Geology, in which different strata cross one another or meet at an angle, or mix with one another either by slow transitions or by violent convulsions, leaving many lacunae which can be no longer filled up, and often becoming so complex that no true explanation of them can be given.</div><div class="sentence"> So in language there are the cross influences of meaning and sound, of logic and grammar, of differing analogies, of words and the inflexions of words, which often come into conflict with each other.</div><div class="sentence"> The grammarian, if he were to form new words, would make them all of the same pattern according to what he conceives to be the rule, that is, the more common usage of language.</div><div class="sentence"> The subtlety of nature goes far beyond art, and it is complicated by irregularity, so that often we can hardly say that there is a right or wrong in the formation of words.</div><div class="sentence"> For almost any formation which is not at variance with the first principles of language is possible and may be defended.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">098</span> <div class="sentence">  The imperfection of language is really due to the formation and correlation of words by accident, that is to say, by principles which are unknown to us.</div><div class="sentence"> Hence we see why Plato, like ourselves unable to comprehend the whole of language, was constrained to “supplement the poor creature imitation by another poor creature convention.” But the poor creature convention in the end proves too much for all the rest: for we do not ask what is the origin of words or whether they are formed according to a correct analogy, but what is the usage of them; and we are compelled to admit with Hermogenes in Plato and with Horace that usage is the ruling principle, “quem penes arbitrium est, et jus et norma loquendi.”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">099</span> <div class="sentence">  (8) There are two ways in which a language may attain permanence or fixity.</div><div class="sentence"> First, it may have been embodied in poems or hymns or laws, which may be repeated for hundreds, perhaps for thousands of years with a religious accuracy, so that to the priests or rhapsodists of a nation the whole or the greater part of a language is literally preserved; secondly, it may be written down and in a written form distributed more or less widely among the whole nation.</div><div class="sentence"> In either case the language which is familiarly spoken may have grown up wholly or in a great measure independently of them.</div><div class="sentence"> (1) The first of these processes has been sometimes attended by the result that the sound of the words has been carefully preserved and that the meaning of them has either perished wholly, or is only doubtfully recovered by the efforts of modern philology.</div><div class="sentence"> The verses have been repeated as a chant or part of a ritual, but they have had no relation to ordinary life or speech.</div><div class="sentence"> (2) The invention of writing again is commonly attributed to a particular epoch, and we are apt to think that such an inestimable gift would have immediately been diffused over a whole country.</div><div class="sentence"> But it may have taken a long time to perfect the art of writing, and another long period may have elapsed before it came into common use.</div><div class="sentence"> Its influence on language has been increased ten, twenty or one hundred fold by the invention of printing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">100</span> <div class="sentence">  Before the growth of poetry or the invention of writing, languages were only dialects.</div><div class="sentence"> So they continued to be in parts of the country in which writing was not used or in which there was no diffusion of literature.</div><div class="sentence"> In most of the counties of England there is still a provincial style, which has been sometimes made by a great poet the vehicle of his fancies.</div><div class="sentence"> When a book sinks into the mind of a nation, such as Luther’s Bible or the Authorized English Translation of the Bible, or again great classical works like Shakspere or Milton, not only have new powers of expression been diffused through a whole nation, but a great step towards uniformity has been made.</div><div class="sentence"> The instinct of language demands regular grammar and correct spelling: these are imprinted deeply on the tablets of a nation’s memory by a common use of classical and popular writers.</div><div class="sentence"> In our own day we have attained to a point at which nearly every printed book is spelt correctly and written grammatically.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">101</span> <div class="sentence">  (9) Proceeding further to trace the influence of literature on language we note some other causes which have affected the higher use of it: such as (1) the necessity of clearness and connexion; (2) the fear of tautology; (3) the influence of metre, rhythm, rhyme, and of the language of prose and verse upon one another; (4) the power of idiom and quotation; (5) the relativeness of words to one another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">102</span> <div class="sentence">  It has been usual to depreciate modern languages when compared with ancient.</div><div class="sentence"> The latter are regarded as furnishing a type of excellence to which the former cannot attain.</div><div class="sentence"> But the truth seems to be that modern languages, if through the loss of inflections and genders they lack some power or beauty or expressiveness or precision which is possessed by the ancient, are in many other respects superior to them: the thought is generally clearer, the connexion closer, the sentence and paragraph are better distributed.</div><div class="sentence"> The best modern languages, for example English or French, possess as great a power of self-improvement as the Latin, if not as the Greek.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor does there seem to be any reason why they should ever decline or decay.</div><div class="sentence"> It is a popular remark that our great writers are beginning to disappear: it may also be remarked that whenever a great writer appears in the future he will find the English language as perfect and as ready for use as in the days of Shakspere or Milton.</div><div class="sentence"> There is no reason to suppose that English or French will ever be reduced to the low level of Modern Greek or of Mediaeval Latin.</div><div class="sentence"> The wide diffusion of great authors would make such a decline impossible.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor will modern languages be easily broken up by amalgamation with each other.</div><div class="sentence"> The distance between them is too wide to be spanned, the differences are too great to be overcome, and the use of printing makes it impossible that one of them should ever be lost in another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">103</span> <div class="sentence">  The structure of the English language differs greatly from that of either Latin or Greek.</div><div class="sentence"> In the two latter, especially in Greek, sentences are joined together by connecting particles.</div><div class="sentence"> They are distributed on the right hand and on the left by men, de, alla, kaitoi, kai de and the like, or deduced from one another by ara, de, oun, toinun and the like.</div><div class="sentence"> In English the majority of sentences are independent and in apposition to one another; they are laid side by side or slightly connected by the copula.</div><div class="sentence"> But within the sentence the expression of the logical relations of the clauses is closer and more exact: there is less of apposition and participial structure.</div><div class="sentence"> The sentences thus laid side by side are also constructed into paragraphs; these again are less distinctly marked in Greek and Latin than in English.</div><div class="sentence"> Generally French, German, and English have an advantage over the classical languages in point of accuracy.</div><div class="sentence"> The three concords are more accurately observed in English than in either Greek or Latin.</div><div class="sentence"> On the other hand, the extension of the familiar use of the masculine and feminine gender to objects of sense and abstract ideas as well as to men and animals no doubt lends a nameless grace to style which we have a difficulty in appreciating, and the possible variety in the order of words gives more flexibility and also a kind of dignity to the period.</div><div class="sentence"> Of the comparative effect of accent and quantity and of the relation between them in ancient and modern languages we are not able to judge.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">104</span> <div class="sentence">  Another quality in which modern are superior to ancient languages is freedom from tautology.</div><div class="sentence"> No English style is thought tolerable in which, except for the sake of emphasis, the same words are repeated at short intervals.</div><div class="sentence"> Of course the length of the interval must depend on the character of the word.</div><div class="sentence"> Striking words and expressions cannot be allowed to reappear, if at all, except at the distance of a page or more.</div><div class="sentence"> Pronouns, prepositions, conjunctions may or rather must recur in successive lines.</div><div class="sentence"> It seems to be a kind of impertinence to the reader and strikes unpleasantly both on the mind and on the ear that the same sounds should be used twice over, when another word or turn of expression would have given a new shade of meaning to the thought and would have added a pleasing variety to the sound.</div><div class="sentence"> And the mind equally rejects the repetition of the word and the use of a mere synonym for it,—e.g.</div><div class="sentence"> felicity and happiness.</div><div class="sentence"> The cultivated mind desires something more, which a skilful writer is easily able to supply out of his treasure-house.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">105</span> <div class="sentence">  The fear of tautology has doubtless led to the multiplications of words and the meanings of words, and generally to an enlargement of the vocabulary.</div><div class="sentence"> It is a very early instinct of language; for ancient poetry is almost as free from tautology as the best modern writings.</div><div class="sentence"> The speech of young children, except in so far as they are compelled to repeat themselves by the fewness of their words, also escapes from it.</div><div class="sentence"> When they grow up and have ideas which are beyond their powers of expression, especially in writing, tautology begins to appear.</div><div class="sentence"> In like manner when language is “contaminated” by philosophy it is apt to become awkward, to stammer and repeat itself, to lose its flow and freedom.</div><div class="sentence"> No philosophical writer with the exception of Plato, who is himself not free from tautology, and perhaps Bacon, has attained to any high degree of literary excellence.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">106</span> <div class="sentence">  To poetry the form and polish of language is chiefly to be attributed; and the most critical period in the history of language is the transition from verse to prose.</div><div class="sentence"> At first mankind were contented to express their thoughts in a set form of words having a kind of rhythm; to which regularity was given by accent and quantity.</div><div class="sentence"> But after a time they demanded a greater degree of freedom, and to those who had all their life been hearing poetry the first introduction of prose had the charm of novelty.</div><div class="sentence"> The prose romances into which the Homeric Poems were converted, for a while probably gave more delight to the hearers or readers of them than the Poems themselves, and in time the relation of the two was reversed: the poems which had once been a necessity of the human mind became a luxury: they were now superseded by prose, which in all succeeding ages became the natural vehicle of expression to all mankind.</div><div class="sentence"> Henceforward prose and poetry formed each other.</div><div class="sentence"> A comparatively slender link between them was also furnished by proverbs.</div><div class="sentence"> We may trace in poetry how the simple succession of lines, not without monotony, has passed into a complicated period, and how in prose, rhythm and accent and the order of words and the balance of clauses, sometimes not without a slight admixture of rhyme, make up a new kind of harmony, swelling into strains not less majestic than those of Homer, Virgil, or Dante.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">107</span> <div class="sentence">  One of the most curious and characteristic features of language, affecting both syntax and style, is idiom.</div><div class="sentence"> The meaning of the word “idiom” is that which is peculiar, that which is familiar, the word or expression which strikes us or comes home to us, which is more readily understood or more easily remembered.</div><div class="sentence"> It is a quality which really exists in infinite degrees, which we turn into differences of kind by applying the term only to conspicuous and striking examples of words or phrases which have this quality.</div><div class="sentence"> It often supersedes the laws of language or the rules of grammar, or rather is to be regarded as another law of language which is natural and necessary.</div><div class="sentence"> The word or phrase which has been repeated many times over is more intelligible and familiar to us than one which is rare, and our familiarity with it more than compensates for incorrectness or inaccuracy in the use of it.</div><div class="sentence"> Striking expressions also which have moved the hearts of nations or are the precious stones and jewels of great authors partake of the nature of idioms: they are taken out of the sphere of grammar and are exempt from the proprieties of language.</div><div class="sentence"> Every one knows that we often put words together in a manner which would be intolerable if it were not idiomatic.</div><div class="sentence"> We cannot argue either about the meaning of words or the use of constructions that because they are used in one connexion they will be legitimate in another, unless we allow for this principle.</div><div class="sentence"> We can bear to have words and sentences used in new senses or in a new order or even a little perverted in meaning when we are quite familiar with them.</div><div class="sentence"> Quotations are as often applied in a sense which the author did not intend as in that which he did.</div><div class="sentence"> The parody of the words of Shakspere or of the Bible, which has in it something of the nature of a lie, is far from unpleasing to us.</div><div class="sentence"> The better known words, even if their meaning be perverted, are more agreeable to us and have a greater power over us.</div><div class="sentence"> Most of us have experienced a sort of delight and feeling of curiosity when we first came across or when we first used for ourselves a new word or phrase or figure of speech.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">108</span> <div class="sentence">  There are associations of sound and of sense by which every word is linked to every other.</div><div class="sentence"> One letter harmonizes with another; every verb or noun derives its meaning, not only from itself, but from the words with which it is associated.</div><div class="sentence"> Some reflection of them near or distant is embodied in it.</div><div class="sentence"> In any new use of a word all the existing uses of it have to be considered.</div><div class="sentence"> Upon these depends the question whether it will bear the proposed extension of meaning or not.</div><div class="sentence"> According to the famous expression of Luther, “Words are living creatures, having hands and feet.” When they cease to retain this living power of adaptation, when they are only put together like the parts of a piece of furniture, language becomes unpoetical, inexpressive, dead.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">109</span> <div class="sentence">  Grammars would lead us to suppose that words have a fixed form and sound.</div><div class="sentence"> Lexicons assign to each word a definite meaning or meanings.</div><div class="sentence"> They both tend to obscure the fact that the sentence precedes the word and that all language is relative.</div><div class="sentence"> (1) It is relative to its own context.</div><div class="sentence"> Its meaning is modified by what has been said before and after in the same or in some other passage: without comparing the context we are not sure whether it is used in the same sense even in two successive sentences.</div><div class="sentence"> (2) It is relative to facts, to time, place, and occasion: when they are already known to the hearer or reader, they may be presupposed; there is no need to allude to them further.</div><div class="sentence"> (3) It is relative to the knowledge of the writer and reader or of the speaker and hearer.</div><div class="sentence"> Except for the sake of order and consecutiveness nothing ought to be expressed which is already commonly or universally known.</div><div class="sentence"> A word or two may be sufficient to give an intimation to a friend; a long or elaborate speech or composition is required to explain some new idea to a popular audience or to the ordinary reader or to a young pupil.</div><div class="sentence"> Grammars and dictionaries are not to be despised; for in teaching we need clearness rather than subtlety.</div><div class="sentence"> But we must not therefore forget that there is also a higher ideal of language in which all is relative—sounds to sounds, words to words, the parts to the whole—in which besides the lesser context of the book or speech, there is also the larger context of history and circumstances.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">110</span> <div class="sentence">  The study of Comparative Philology has introduced into the world a new science which more than any other binds up man with nature, and distant ages and countries with one another.</div><div class="sentence"> It may be said to have thrown a light upon all other sciences and upon the nature of the human mind itself.</div><div class="sentence"> The true conception of it dispels many errors, not only of metaphysics and theology, but also of natural knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet it is far from certain that this newly-found science will continue to progress in the same surprising manner as heretofore; or that even if our materials are largely increased, we shall arrive at much more definite conclusions than at present.</div><div class="sentence"> Like some other branches of knowledge, it may be approaching a point at which it can no longer be profitably studied.</div><div class="sentence"> But at any rate it has brought back the philosophy of language from theory to fact; it has passed out of the region of guesses and hypotheses, and has attained the dignity of an Inductive Science.</div><div class="sentence"> And it is not without practical and political importance.</div><div class="sentence"> It gives a new interest to distant and subject countries; it brings back the dawning light from one end of the earth to the other.</div><div class="sentence"> Nations, like individuals, are better understood by us when we know something of their early life; and when they are better understood by us, we feel more kindly towards them.</div><div class="sentence"> Lastly, we may remember that all knowledge is valuable for its own sake; and we may also hope that a deeper insight into the nature of human speech will give us a greater command of it and enable us to make a nobler use of it.</div><div class="sentence"> [2]</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">111</span> <div class="sentence">  [2] Compare again W. Humboldt, Ueber die Verschiedenheit des menschlichen Sprachbaues; M. Müller, Lectures on the Science of Language; Steinthal, Einleitung in die Psychologie und Sprachwissenschaft: and for the latter part of the Essay, Delbruck, Study of Language; Paul’s Principles of the History of Language: to the latter work the author of this Essay is largely indebted.</div></div>

</div><div id="chap02" class="book_section">
<h2>CRATYLUS</h2>

<div class="speech"><span class="ref">001</span> <div class="sentence">  Translated by Benjamin Jowett</div></div>

<div class="description">
<div class="book_description">PERSONS OF THE DIALOGUE:</div> Socrates, Hermogenes, Cratylus. 
</div><div class="speech"><span class="ref">002</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Suppose that we make Socrates a party to the argument?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">003</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  If you please.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">004</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should explain to you, Socrates, that our friend Cratylus has been arguing about names; he says that they are natural and not conventional; not a portion of the human voice which men agree to use; but that there is a truth or correctness in them, which is the same for Hellenes as for barbarians.</div><div class="sentence"> Whereupon I ask him, whether his own name of Cratylus is a true name or not, and he answers “Yes.” And Socrates?</div><div class="sentence"> “Yes.” Then every man’s name, as I tell him, is that which he is called.</div><div class="sentence"> To this he replies—“If all the world were to call you Hermogenes, that would not be your name.” And when I am anxious to have a further explanation he is ironical and mysterious, and seems to imply that he has a notion of his own about the matter, if he would only tell, and could entirely convince me, if he chose to be intelligible.</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me, Socrates, what this oracle means; or rather tell me, if you will be so good, what is your own view of the truth or correctness of names, which I would far sooner hear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">005</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Son of Hipponicus, there is an ancient saying, that “hard is the knowledge of the good.” And the knowledge of names is a great part of knowledge.</div><div class="sentence"> If I had not been poor, I might have heard the fifty-drachma course of the great Prodicus, which is a complete education in grammar and language—these are his own words—and then I should have been at once able to answer your question about the correctness of names.</div><div class="sentence"> But, indeed, I have only heard the single-drachma course, and therefore, I do not know the truth about such matters; I will, however, gladly assist you and Cratylus in the investigation of them.</div><div class="sentence"> When he declares that your name is not really Hermogenes, I suspect that he is only making fun of you;—he means to say that you are no true son of Hermes, because you are always looking after a fortune and never in luck.</div><div class="sentence"> But, as I was saying, there is a good deal of difficulty in this sort of knowledge, and therefore we had better leave the question open until we have heard both sides.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">006</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I have often talked over this matter, both with Cratylus and others, and cannot convince myself that there is any principle of correctness in names other than convention and agreement; any name which you give, in my opinion, is the right one, and if you change that and give another, the new name is as correct as the old—we frequently change the names of our slaves, and the newly-imposed name is as good as the old:  for there is no name given to anything by nature; all is convention and habit of the users;—such is my view.</div><div class="sentence"> But if I am mistaken I shall be happy to hear and learn of Cratylus, or of any one else.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">007</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I dare say that you may be right, Hermogenes:  let us see;—Your meaning is, that the name of each thing is only that which anybody agrees to call it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">008</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is my notion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">009</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Whether the giver of the name be an individual or a city?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">010</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">011</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, now, let me take an instance;—suppose that I call a man a horse or a horse a man, you mean to say that a man will be rightly called a horse by me individually, and rightly called a man by the rest of the world; and a horse again would be rightly called a man by me and a horse by the world: —that is your meaning?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">012</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  He would, according to my view.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">013</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But how about truth, then?</div><div class="sentence"> you would acknowledge that there is in words a true and a false?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">014</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">015</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And there are true and false propositions?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">016</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">017</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And a true proposition says that which is, and a false proposition says that which is not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">018</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; what other answer is possible?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">019</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then in a proposition there is a true and false?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">020</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">021</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But is a proposition true as a whole only, and are the parts untrue?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">022</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No; the parts are true as well as the whole.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">023</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Would you say the large parts and not the smaller ones, or every part?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">024</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that every part is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">025</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Is a proposition resolvable into any part smaller than a name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">026</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No; that is the smallest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">027</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the name is a part of the true proposition?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">028</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">029</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, and a true part, as you say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">030</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">031</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not the part of a falsehood also a falsehood?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">032</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">033</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, if propositions may be true and false, names may be true and false?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">034</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  So we must infer.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">035</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the name of anything is that which any one affirms to be the name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">036</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">037</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And will there be so many names of each thing as everybody says that there are?</div><div class="sentence"> and will they be true names at the time of uttering them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">038</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, I can conceive no correctness of names other than this; you give one name, and I another; and in different cities and countries there are different names for the same things; Hellenes differ from barbarians in their use of names, and the several Hellenic tribes from one another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">039</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But would you say, Hermogenes, that the things differ as the names differ?</div><div class="sentence"> and are they relative to individuals, as Protagoras tells us?</div><div class="sentence"> For he says that man is the measure of all things, and that things are to me as they appear to me, and that they are to you as they appear to you.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you agree with him, or would you say that things have a permanent essence of their own?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">040</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There have been times, Socrates, when I have been driven in my perplexity to take refuge with Protagoras; not that I agree with him at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">041</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What!</div><div class="sentence"> have you ever been driven to admit that there was no such thing as a bad man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">042</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed; but I have often had reason to think that there are very bad men, and a good many of them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">043</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and have you ever found any very good ones?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">044</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Not many.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">045</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Still you have found them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">046</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">047</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you hold that the very good were the very wise, and the very evil very foolish?</div><div class="sentence"> Would that be your view?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">048</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  It would.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">049</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if Protagoras is right, and the truth is that things are as they appear to any one, how can some of us be wise and some of us foolish?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">050</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Impossible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">051</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if, on the other hand, wisdom and folly are really distinguishable, you will allow, I think, that the assertion of Protagoras can hardly be correct.</div><div class="sentence"> For if what appears to each man is true to him, one man cannot in reality be wiser than another.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">052</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  He cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">053</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nor will you be disposed to say with Euthydemus, that all things equally belong to all men at the same moment and always; for neither on his view can there be some good and others bad, if virtue and vice are always equally to be attributed to all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">054</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">055</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if neither is right, and things are not relative to individuals, and all things do not equally belong to all at the same moment and always, they must be supposed to have their own proper and permanent essence:  they are not in relation to us, or influenced by us, fluctuating according to our fancy, but they are independent, and maintain to their own essence the relation prescribed by nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">056</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think, Socrates, that you have said the truth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">057</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Does what I am saying apply only to the things themselves, or equally to the actions which proceed from them?</div><div class="sentence"> Are not actions also a class of being?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">058</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, the actions are real as well as the things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">059</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the actions also are done according to their proper nature, and not according to our opinion of them?</div><div class="sentence"> In cutting, for example, we do not cut as we please, and with any chance instrument; but we cut with the proper instrument only, and according to the natural process of cutting; and the natural process is right and will succeed, but any other will fail and be of no use at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">060</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that the natural way is the right way.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">061</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, in burning, not every way is the right way; but the right way is the natural way, and the right instrument the natural instrument.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">062</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">063</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And this holds good of all actions?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">064</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">065</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And speech is a kind of action?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">066</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">067</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And will a man speak correctly who speaks as he pleases?</div><div class="sentence"> Will not the successful speaker rather be he who speaks in the natural way of speaking, and as things ought to be spoken, and with the natural instrument?</div><div class="sentence"> Any other mode of speaking will result in error and failure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">068</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I quite agree with you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">069</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not naming a part of speaking?</div><div class="sentence"> for in giving names men speak.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">070</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">071</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if speaking is a sort of action and has a relation to acts, is not naming also a sort of action?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">072</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">073</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And we saw that actions were not relative to ourselves, but had a special nature of their own?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">074</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Precisely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">075</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the argument would lead us to infer that names ought to be given according to a natural process, and with a proper instrument, and not at our pleasure:  in this and no other way shall we name with success.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">076</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">077</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But again, that which has to be cut has to be cut with something?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">078</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">079</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that which has to be woven or pierced has to be woven or pierced with something?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">080</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">081</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that which has to be named has to be named with something?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">082</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">083</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What is that with which we pierce?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">084</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  An awl.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">085</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And with which we weave?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">086</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  A shuttle.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">087</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And with which we name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">088</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  A name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">089</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good:  then a name is an instrument?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">090</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">091</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Suppose that I ask, “What sort of instrument is a shuttle?” And you answer, “A weaving instrument.”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">092</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Well.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">093</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And I ask again, “What do we do when we weave?”—The answer is, that we separate or disengage the warp from the woof.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">094</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">095</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And may not a similar description be given of an awl, and of instruments in general?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">096</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">097</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And now suppose that I ask a similar question about names:  will you answer me?</div><div class="sentence"> Regarding the name as an instrument, what do we do when we name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">098</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">099</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do we not give information to one another, and distinguish things according to their natures?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">100</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly we do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">101</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then a name is an instrument of teaching and of distinguishing natures, as the shuttle is of distinguishing the threads of the web.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">102</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">103</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the shuttle is the instrument of the weaver?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">104</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Assuredly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">105</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the weaver will use the shuttle well—and well means like a weaver?</div><div class="sentence"> and the teacher will use the name well—and well means like a teacher?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">106</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">107</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when the weaver uses the shuttle, whose work will he be using well?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">108</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That of the carpenter.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">109</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is every man a carpenter, or the skilled only?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">110</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Only the skilled.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">111</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when the piercer uses the awl, whose work will he be using well?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">112</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That of the smith.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">113</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is every man a smith, or only the skilled?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">114</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  The skilled only.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">115</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when the teacher uses the name, whose work will he be using?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">116</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There again I am puzzled.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">117</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Cannot you at least say who gives us the names which we use?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">118</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed I cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">119</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Does not the law seem to you to give us them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">120</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I suppose so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">121</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the teacher, when he gives us a name, uses the work of the legislator?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">122</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">123</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is every man a legislator, or the skilled only?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">124</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  The skilled only.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">125</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, Hermogenes, not every man is able to give a name, but only a maker of names; and this is the legislator, who of all skilled artisans in the world is the rarest.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">126</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">127</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And how does the legislator make names?</div><div class="sentence"> and to what does he look?</div><div class="sentence"> Consider this in the light of the previous instances:  to what does the carpenter look in making the shuttle?</div><div class="sentence"> Does he not look to that which is naturally fitted to act as a shuttle?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">128</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">129</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And suppose the shuttle to be broken in making, will he make another, looking to the broken one?</div><div class="sentence"> or will he look to the form according to which he made the other?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">130</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To the latter, I should imagine.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">131</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Might not that be justly called the true or ideal shuttle?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">132</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">133</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And whatever shuttles are wanted, for the manufacture of garments, thin or thick, of flaxen, woollen, or other material, ought all of them to have the true form of the shuttle; and whatever is the shuttle best adapted to each kind of work, that ought to be the form which the maker produces in each case.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">134</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">135</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the same holds of other instruments:  when a man has discovered the instrument which is naturally adapted to each work, he must express this natural form, and not others which he fancies, in the material, whatever it may be, which he employs; for example, he ought to know how to put into iron the forms of awls adapted by nature to their several uses?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">136</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">137</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And how to put into wood forms of shuttles adapted by nature to their uses?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">138</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">139</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  For the several forms of shuttles naturally answer to the several kinds of webs; and this is true of instruments in general.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">140</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">141</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, as to names:  ought not our legislator also to know how to put the true natural name of each thing into sounds and syllables, and to make and give all names with a view to the ideal name, if he is to be a namer in any true sense?</div><div class="sentence"> And we must remember that different legislators will not use the same syllables.</div><div class="sentence"> For neither does every smith, although he may be making the same instrument for the same purpose, make them all of the same iron.</div><div class="sentence"> The form must be the same, but the material may vary, and still the instrument may be equally good of whatever iron made, whether in Hellas or in a foreign country;—there is no difference.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">142</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">143</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the legislator, whether he be Hellene or barbarian, is not therefore to be deemed by you a worse legislator, provided he gives the true and proper form of the name in whatever syllables; this or that country makes no matter.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">144</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">145</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But who then is to determine whether the proper form is given to the shuttle, whatever sort of wood may be used?</div><div class="sentence"> the carpenter who makes, or the weaver who is to use them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">146</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should say, he who is to use them, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">147</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who uses the work of the lyre-maker?</div><div class="sentence"> Will not he be the man who knows how to direct what is being done, and who will know also whether the work is being well done or not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">148</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">149</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who is he?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">150</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  The player of the lyre.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">151</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who will direct the shipwright?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">152</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  The pilot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">153</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who will be best able to direct the legislator in his work, and will know whether the work is well done, in this or any other country?</div><div class="sentence"> Will not the user be the man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">154</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">155</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And this is he who knows how to ask questions?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">156</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">157</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And how to answer them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">158</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">159</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And him who knows how to ask and answer you would call a dialectician?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">160</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; that would be his name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">161</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the work of the carpenter is to make a rudder, and the pilot has to direct him, if the rudder is to be well made.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">162</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">163</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the work of the legislator is to give names, and the dialectician must be his director if the names are to be rightly given?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">164</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">165</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, Hermogenes, I should say that this giving of names can be no such light matter as you fancy, or the work of light or chance persons; and Cratylus is right in saying that things have names by nature, and that not every man is an artificer of names, but he only who looks to the name which each thing by nature has, and is able to express the true forms of things in letters and syllables.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">166</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I cannot answer you, Socrates; but I find a difficulty in changing my opinion all in a moment, and I think that I should be more readily persuaded, if you would show me what this is which you term the natural fitness of names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">167</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My good Hermogenes, I have none to show.</div><div class="sentence"> Was I not telling you just now (but you have forgotten), that I knew nothing, and proposing to share the enquiry with you?</div><div class="sentence"> But now that you and I have talked over the matter, a step has been gained; for we have discovered that names have by nature a truth, and that not every man knows how to give a thing a name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">168</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">169</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what is the nature of this truth or correctness of names?</div><div class="sentence"> That, if you care to know, is the next question.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">170</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, I care to know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">171</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then reflect.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">172</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How shall I reflect?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">173</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The true way is to have the assistance of those who know, and you must pay them well both in money and in thanks; these are the Sophists, of whom your brother, Callias, has—rather dearly—bought the reputation of wisdom.</div><div class="sentence"> But you have not yet come into your inheritance, and therefore you had better go to him, and beg and entreat him to tell you what he has learnt from Protagoras about the fitness of names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">174</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But how inconsistent should I be, if, whilst repudiating Protagoras and his truth (“Truth” was the title of the book of Protagoras; compare Theaet.</div><div class="sentence"> ), I were to attach any value to what he and his book affirm!</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">175</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then if you despise him, you must learn of Homer and the poets.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">176</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  And where does Homer say anything about names, and what does he say?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">177</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He often speaks of them; notably and nobly in the places where he distinguishes the different names which Gods and men give to the same things.</div><div class="sentence"> Does he not in these passages make a remarkable statement about the correctness of names?</div><div class="sentence"> For the Gods must clearly be supposed to call things by their right and natural names; do you not think so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">178</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, of course they call them rightly, if they call them at all.</div><div class="sentence"> But to what are you referring?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">179</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you not know what he says about the river in Troy who had a single combat with Hephaestus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">180</span> <div class="sentence">  “Whom,” as he says, “the Gods call Xanthus, and men call Scamander.”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">181</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I remember.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">182</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and about this river—to know that he ought to be called Xanthus and not Scamander—is not that a solemn lesson?</div><div class="sentence"> Or about the bird which, as he says,</div></div>
