<div class="speech"><span class="ref">183</span> <div class="sentence">  “The Gods call Chalcis, and men Cymindis:”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">184</span> <div class="sentence">  to be taught how much more correct the name Chalcis is than the name Cymindis—do you deem that a light matter?</div><div class="sentence"> Or about Batieia and Myrina?</div><div class="sentence"> (Compare Il.</div><div class="sentence"> “The hill which men call Batieia and the immortals the tomb of the sportive Myrina.”) And there are many other observations of the same kind in Homer and other poets.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, I think that this is beyond the understanding of you and me; but the names of Scamandrius and Astyanax, which he affirms to have been the names of Hector’s son, are more within the range of human faculties, as I am disposed to think; and what the poet means by correctness may be more readily apprehended in that instance: you will remember I dare say the lines to which I refer?</div><div class="sentence"> (Il.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">185</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">186</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me ask you, then, which did Homer think the more correct of the names given to Hector’s son—Astyanax or Scamandrius?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">187</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I do not know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">188</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How would you answer, if you were asked whether the wise or the unwise are more likely to give correct names?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">189</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should say the wise, of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">190</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are the men or the women of a city, taken as a class, the wiser?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">191</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should say, the men.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">192</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And Homer, as you know, says that the Trojan men called him Astyanax (king of the city); but if the men called him Astyanax, the other name of Scamandrius could only have been given to him by the women.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">193</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That may be inferred.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">194</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And must not Homer have imagined the Trojans to be wiser than their wives?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">195</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">196</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then he must have thought Astyanax to be a more correct name for the boy than Scamandrius?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">197</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">198</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what is the reason of this?</div><div class="sentence"> Let us consider: —does he not himself suggest a very good reason, when he says,</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">199</span> <div class="sentence">  “For he alone defended their city and long walls”?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">200</span> <div class="sentence">  This appears to be a good reason for calling the son of the saviour king of the city which his father was saving, as Homer observes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">201</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I see.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">202</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, Hermogenes, I do not as yet see myself; and do you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">203</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed; not I.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">204</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But tell me, friend, did not Homer himself also give Hector his name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">205</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What of that?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">206</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The name appears to me to be very nearly the same as the name of Astyanax—both are Hellenic; and a king (anax) and a holder (ektor) have nearly the same meaning, and are both descriptive of a king; for a man is clearly the holder of that of which he is king; he rules, and owns, and holds it.</div><div class="sentence"> But, perhaps, you may think that I am talking nonsense; and indeed I believe that I myself did not know what I meant when I imagined that I had found some indication of the opinion of Homer about the correctness of names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">207</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I assure you that I think otherwise, and I believe you to be on the right track.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">208</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is reason, I think, in calling the lion’s whelp a lion, and the foal of a horse a horse; I am speaking only of the ordinary course of nature, when an animal produces after his kind, and not of extraordinary births;—if contrary to nature a horse have a calf, then I should not call that a foal but a calf; nor do I call any inhuman birth a man, but only a natural birth.</div><div class="sentence"> And the same may be said of trees and other things.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you agree with me?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">209</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">210</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div><div class="sentence"> But you had better watch me and see that I do not play tricks with you.</div><div class="sentence"> For on the same principle the son of a king is to be called a king.</div><div class="sentence"> And whether the syllables of the name are the same or not the same, makes no difference, provided the meaning is retained; nor does the addition or subtraction of a letter make any difference so long as the essence of the thing remains in possession of the name and appears in it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">211</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">212</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  A very simple matter.</div><div class="sentence"> I may illustrate my meaning by the names of letters, which you know are not the same as the letters themselves with the exception of the four epsilon, upsilon, omicron, omega; the names of the rest, whether vowels or consonants, are made up of other letters which we add to them; but so long as we introduce the meaning, and there can be no mistake, the name of the letter is quite correct.</div><div class="sentence"> Take, for example, the letter beta—the addition of eta, tau, alpha, gives no offence, and does not prevent the whole name from having the value which the legislator intended—so well did he know how to give the letters names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">213</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I believe you are right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">214</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And may not the same be said of a king?</div><div class="sentence"> a king will often be the son of a king, the good son or the noble son of a good or noble sire; and similarly the offspring of every kind, in the regular course of nature, is like the parent, and therefore has the same name.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet the syllables may be disguised until they appear different to the ignorant person, and he may not recognize them, although they are the same, just as any one of us would not recognize the same drugs under different disguises of colour and smell, although to the physician, who regards the power of them, they are the same, and he is not put out by the addition; and in like manner the etymologist is not put out by the addition or transposition or subtraction of a letter or two, or indeed by the change of all the letters, for this need not interfere with the meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> As was just now said, the names of Hector and Astyanax have only one letter alike, which is tau, and yet they have the same meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> And how little in common with the letters of their names has Archepolis (ruler of the city)—and yet the meaning is the same.</div><div class="sentence"> And there are many other names which just mean “king.” Again, there are several names for a general, as, for example, Agis (leader) and Polemarchus (chief in war) and Eupolemus (good warrior); and others which denote a physician, as Iatrocles (famous healer) and Acesimbrotus (curer of mortals); and there are many others which might be cited, differing in their syllables and letters, but having the same meaning.</div><div class="sentence"> Would you not say so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">215</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">216</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The same names, then, ought to be assigned to those who follow in the course of nature?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">217</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">218</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what of those who follow out of the course of nature, and are prodigies?</div><div class="sentence"> for example, when a good and religious man has an irreligious son, he ought to bear the name not of his father, but of the class to which he belongs, just as in the case which was before supposed of a horse foaling a calf.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">219</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">220</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the irreligious son of a religious father should be called irreligious?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">221</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">222</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He should not be called Theophilus (beloved of God) or Mnesitheus (mindful of God), or any of these names:  if names are correctly given, his should have an opposite meaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">223</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">224</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, Hermogenes, there is Orestes (the man of the mountains) who appears to be rightly called; whether chance gave the name, or perhaps some poet who meant to express the brutality and fierceness and mountain wildness of his hero’s nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">225</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is very likely, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">226</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And his father’s name is also according to nature.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">227</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">228</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, for as his name, so also is his nature; Agamemnon (admirable for remaining) is one who is patient and persevering in the accomplishment of his resolves, and by his virtue crowns them; and his continuance at Troy with all the vast army is a proof of that admirable endurance in him which is signified by the name Agamemnon.</div><div class="sentence"> I also think that Atreus is rightly called; for his murder of Chrysippus and his exceeding cruelty to Thyestes are damaging and destructive to his reputation—the name is a little altered and disguised so as not to be intelligible to every one, but to the etymologist there is no difficulty in seeing the meaning, for whether you think of him as ateires the stubborn, or as atrestos the fearless, or as ateros the destructive one, the name is perfectly correct in every point of view.</div><div class="sentence"> And I think that Pelops is also named appropriately; for, as the name implies, he is rightly called Pelops who sees what is near only (o ta pelas oron).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">229</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">230</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Because, according to the tradition, he had no forethought or foresight of all the evil which the murder of Myrtilus would entail upon his whole race in remote ages; he saw only what was at hand and immediate,—or in other words, pelas (near), in his eagerness to win Hippodamia by all means for his bride.</div><div class="sentence"> Every one would agree that the name of Tantalus is rightly given and in accordance with nature, if the traditions about him are true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">231</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  And what are the traditions?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">232</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Many terrible misfortunes are said to have happened to him in his life—last of all, came the utter ruin of his country; and after his death he had the stone suspended (talanteia) over his head in the world below—all this agrees wonderfully well with his name.</div><div class="sentence"> You might imagine that some person who wanted to call him Talantatos (the most weighted down by misfortune), disguised the name by altering it into Tantalus; and into this form, by some accident of tradition, it has actually been transmuted.</div><div class="sentence"> The name of Zeus, who is his alleged father, has also an excellent meaning, although hard to be understood, because really like a sentence, which is divided into two parts, for some call him Zena, and use the one half, and others who use the other half call him Dia; the two together signify the nature of the God, and the business of a name, as we were saying, is to express the nature.</div><div class="sentence"> For there is none who is more the author of life to us and to all, than the lord and king of all.</div><div class="sentence"> Wherefore we are right in calling him Zena and Dia, which are one name, although divided, meaning the God through whom all creatures always have life (di on zen aei pasi tois zosin uparchei).</div><div class="sentence"> There is an irreverence, at first sight, in calling him son of Cronos (who is a proverb for stupidity), and we might rather expect Zeus to be the child of a mighty intellect.</div><div class="sentence"> Which is the fact; for this is the meaning of his father’s name:  Kronos quasi Koros (Choreo, to sweep), not in the sense of a youth, but signifying to chatharon chai acheraton tou nou, the pure and garnished mind (sc.</div><div class="sentence"> apo tou chorein).</div><div class="sentence"> He, as we are informed by tradition, was begotten of Uranus, rightly so called (apo tou oran ta ano) from looking upwards; which, as philosophers tell us, is the way to have a pure mind, and the name Uranus is therefore correct.</div><div class="sentence"> If I could remember the genealogy of Hesiod, I would have gone on and tried more conclusions of the same sort on the remoter ancestors of the Gods,—then I might have seen whether this wisdom, which has come to me all in an instant, I know not whence, will or will not hold good to the end.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">233</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  You seem to me, Socrates, to be quite like a prophet newly inspired, and to be uttering oracles.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">234</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Hermogenes, and I believe that I caught the inspiration from the great Euthyphro of the Prospaltian deme, who gave me a long lecture which commenced at dawn:  he talked and I listened, and his wisdom and enchanting ravishment has not only filled my ears but taken possession of my soul, and to-day I shall let his superhuman power work and finish the investigation of names—that will be the way; but to-morrow, if you are so disposed, we will conjure him away, and make a purgation of him, if we can only find some priest or sophist who is skilled in purifications of this sort.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">235</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  With all my heart; for am very curious to hear the rest of the enquiry about names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">236</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then let us proceed; and where would you have us begin, now that we have got a sort of outline of the enquiry?</div><div class="sentence"> Are there any names which witness of themselves that they are not given arbitrarily, but have a natural fitness?</div><div class="sentence"> The names of heroes and of men in general are apt to be deceptive because they are often called after ancestors with whose names, as we were saying, they may have no business; or they are the expression of a wish like Eutychides (the son of good fortune), or Sosias (the Saviour), or Theophilus (the beloved of God), and others.</div><div class="sentence"> But I think that we had better leave these, for there will be more chance of finding correctness in the names of immutable essences;—there ought to have been more care taken about them when they were named, and perhaps there may have been some more than human power at work occasionally in giving them names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">237</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think so, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">238</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Ought we not to begin with the consideration of the Gods, and show that they are rightly named Gods?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">239</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that will be well.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">240</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My notion would be something of this sort: —I suspect that the sun, moon, earth, stars, and heaven, which are still the Gods of many barbarians, were the only Gods known to the aboriginal Hellenes.</div><div class="sentence"> Seeing that they were always moving and running, from their running nature they were called Gods or runners (Theous, Theontas); and when men became acquainted with the other Gods, they proceeded to apply the same name to them all.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you think that likely?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">241</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think it very likely indeed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">242</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What shall follow the Gods?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">243</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Must not demons and heroes and men come next?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">244</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Demons!</div><div class="sentence"> And what do you consider to be the meaning of this word?</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me if my view is right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">245</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me hear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">246</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You know how Hesiod uses the word?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">247</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I do not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">248</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you not remember that he speaks of a golden race of men who came first?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">249</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">250</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  He says of them—</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">251</span> <div class="sentence">  “But now that fate has closed over this race They are holy demons upon the earth, Beneficent, averters of ills, guardians of mortal men.” (Hesiod, Works and Days.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">252</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What is the inference?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">253</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What is the inference!</div><div class="sentence"> Why, I suppose that he means by the golden men, not men literally made of gold, but good and noble; and I am convinced of this, because he further says that we are the iron race.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">254</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">255</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you not suppose that good men of our own day would by him be said to be of golden race?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">256</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very likely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">257</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are not the good wise?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">258</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, they are wise.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">259</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And therefore I have the most entire conviction that he called them demons, because they were daemones (knowing or wise), and in our older Attic dialect the word itself occurs.</div><div class="sentence"> Now he and other poets say truly, that when a good man dies he has honour and a mighty portion among the dead, and becomes a demon; which is a name given to him signifying wisdom.</div><div class="sentence"> And I say too, that every wise man who happens to be a good man is more than human (daimonion) both in life and death, and is rightly called a demon.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">260</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Then I rather think that I am of one mind with you; but what is the meaning of the word “hero”?</div><div class="sentence"> (Eros with an eta, in the old writing eros with an epsilon.)</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">261</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I think that there is no difficulty in explaining, for the name is not much altered, and signifies that they were born of love.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">262</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">263</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you not know that the heroes are demigods?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">264</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What then?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">265</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  All of them sprang either from the love of a God for a mortal woman, or of a mortal man for a Goddess; think of the word in the old Attic, and you will see better that the name heros is only a slight alteration of Eros, from whom the heroes sprang:  either this is the meaning, or, if not this, then they must have been skilful as rhetoricians and dialecticians, and able to put the question (erotan), for eirein is equivalent to legein.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore, as I was saying, in the Attic dialect the heroes turn out to be rhetoricians and questioners.</div><div class="sentence"> All this is easy enough; the noble breed of heroes are a tribe of sophists and rhetors.</div><div class="sentence"> But can you tell me why men are called anthropoi?—that is more difficult.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">266</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, I cannot; and I would not try even if I could, because I think that you are the more likely to succeed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">267</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is to say, you trust to the inspiration of Euthyphro.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">268</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">269</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Your faith is not vain; for at this very moment a new and ingenious thought strikes me, and, if I am not careful, before to-morrow’s dawn I shall be wiser than I ought to be.</div><div class="sentence"> Now, attend to me; and first, remember that we often put in and pull out letters in words, and give names as we please and change the accents.</div><div class="sentence"> Take, for example, the word Dii Philos; in order to convert this from a sentence into a noun, we omit one of the iotas and sound the middle syllable grave instead of acute; as, on the other hand, letters are sometimes inserted in words instead of being omitted, and the acute takes the place of the grave.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">270</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">271</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The name anthropos, which was once a sentence, and is now a noun, appears to be a case just of this sort, for one letter, which is the alpha, has been omitted, and the acute on the last syllable has been changed to a grave.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">272</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">273</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I mean to say that the word “man” implies that other animals never examine, or consider, or look up at what they see, but that man not only sees (opope) but considers and looks up at that which he sees, and hence he alone of all animals is rightly anthropos, meaning anathron a opopen.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">274</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  May I ask you to examine another word about which I am curious?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">275</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">276</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I will take that which appears to me to follow next in order.</div><div class="sentence"> You know the distinction of soul and body?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">277</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">278</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us endeavour to analyze them like the previous words.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">279</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You want me first of all to examine the natural fitness of the word psuche (soul), and then of the word soma (body)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">280</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">281</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If I am to say what occurs to me at the moment, I should imagine that those who first used the name psuche meant to express that the soul when in the body is the source of life, and gives the power of breath and revival (anapsuchon), and when this reviving power fails then the body perishes and dies, and this, if I am not mistaken, they called psyche.</div><div class="sentence"> But please stay a moment; I fancy that I can discover something which will be more acceptable to the disciples of Euthyphro, for I am afraid that they will scorn this explanation.</div><div class="sentence"> What do you say to another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">282</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me hear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">283</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What is that which holds and carries and gives life and motion to the entire nature of the body?</div><div class="sentence"> What else but the soul?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">284</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Just that.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">285</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you not believe with Anaxagoras, that mind or soul is the ordering and containing principle of all things?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">286</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">287</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you may well call that power phuseche which carries and holds nature (e phusin okei, kai ekei), and this may be refined away into psuche.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">288</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly; and this derivation is, I think, more scientific than the other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">289</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  It is so; but I cannot help laughing, if I am to suppose that this was the true meaning of the name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">290</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But what shall we say of the next word?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">291</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You mean soma (the body).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">292</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">293</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That may be variously interpreted; and yet more variously if a little permutation is allowed.</div><div class="sentence"> For some say that the body is the grave (sema) of the soul which may be thought to be buried in our present life; or again the index of the soul, because the soul gives indications to (semainei) the body; probably the Orphic poets were the inventors of the name, and they were under the impression that the soul is suffering the punishment of sin, and that the body is an enclosure or prison in which the soul is incarcerated, kept safe (soma, sozetai), as the name soma implies, until the penalty is paid; according to this view, not even a letter of the word need be changed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">294</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think, Socrates, that we have said enough of this class of words.</div><div class="sentence"> But have we any more explanations of the names of the Gods, like that which you were giving of Zeus?</div><div class="sentence"> I should like to know whether any similar principle of correctness is to be applied to them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">295</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, indeed, Hermogenes; and there is one excellent principle which, as men of sense, we must acknowledge,—that of the Gods we know nothing, either of their natures or of the names which they give themselves; but we are sure that the names by which they call themselves, whatever they may be, are true.</div><div class="sentence"> And this is the best of all principles; and the next best is to say, as in prayers, that we will call them by any sort or kind of names or patronymics which they like, because we do not know of any other.</div><div class="sentence"> That also, I think, is a very good custom, and one which I should much wish to observe.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us, then, if you please, in the first place announce to them that we are not enquiring about them; we do not presume that we are able to do so; but we are enquiring about the meaning of men in giving them these names,—in this there can be small blame.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">296</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think, Socrates, that you are quite right, and I would like to do as you say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">297</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall we begin, then, with Hestia, according to custom?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">298</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that will be very proper.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">299</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What may we suppose him to have meant who gave the name Hestia?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">300</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is another and certainly a most difficult question.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">301</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My dear Hermogenes, the first imposers of names must surely have been considerable persons; they were philosophers, and had a good deal to say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">302</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and what of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">303</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  They are the men to whom I should attribute the imposition of names.</div><div class="sentence"> Even in foreign names, if you analyze them, a meaning is still discernible.</div><div class="sentence"> For example, that which we term ousia is by some called esia, and by others again osia.</div><div class="sentence"> Now that the essence of things should be called estia, which is akin to the first of these (esia = estia), is rational enough.</div><div class="sentence"> And there is reason in the Athenians calling that estia which participates in ousia.</div><div class="sentence"> For in ancient times we too seem to have said esia for ousia, and this you may note to have been the idea of those who appointed that sacrifices should be first offered to estia, which was natural enough if they meant that estia was the essence of things.</div><div class="sentence"> Those again who read osia seem to have inclined to the opinion of Heracleitus, that all things flow and nothing stands; with them the pushing principle (othoun) is the cause and ruling power of all things, and is therefore rightly called osia.</div><div class="sentence"> Enough of this, which is all that we who know nothing can affirm.</div><div class="sentence"> Next in order after Hestia we ought to consider Rhea and Cronos, although the name of Cronos has been already discussed.</div><div class="sentence"> But I dare say that I am talking great nonsense.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">304</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">305</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My good friend, I have discovered a hive of wisdom.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">306</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Of what nature?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">307</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, rather ridiculous, and yet plausible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">308</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How plausible?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">309</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I fancy to myself Heracleitus repeating wise traditions of antiquity as old as the days of Cronos and Rhea, and of which Homer also spoke.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">310</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">311</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Heracleitus is supposed to say that all things are in motion and nothing at rest; he compares them to the stream of a river, and says that you cannot go into the same water twice.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">312</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">313</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, how can we avoid inferring that he who gave the names of Cronos and Rhea to the ancestors of the Gods, agreed pretty much in the doctrine of Heracleitus?</div><div class="sentence"> Is the giving of the names of streams to both of them purely accidental?</div><div class="sentence"> Compare the line in which Homer, and, as I believe, Hesiod also, tells of</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">314</span> <div class="sentence">  “Ocean, the origin of Gods, and mother Tethys (Il.—the line is not found in the extant works of Hesiod.</div><div class="sentence"> ).”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">315</span> <div class="sentence">  And again, Orpheus says, that</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">316</span> <div class="sentence">  “The fair river of Ocean was the first to marry, and he espoused his sister Tethys, who was his mother’s daughter.”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">317</span> <div class="sentence">  You see that this is a remarkable coincidence, and all in the direction of Heracleitus.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">318</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think that there is something in what you say, Socrates; but I do not understand the meaning of the name Tethys.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">319</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, that is almost self-explained, being only the name of a spring, a little disguised; for that which is strained and filtered (diattomenon, ethoumenon) may be likened to a spring, and the name Tethys is made up of these two words.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">320</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  The idea is ingenious, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">321</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div><div class="sentence"> But what comes next?—of Zeus we have spoken.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">322</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">323</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then let us next take his two brothers, Poseidon and Pluto, whether the latter is called by that or by his other name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">324</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  By all means.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">325</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Poseidon is Posidesmos, the chain of the feet; the original inventor of the name had been stopped by the watery element in his walks, and not allowed to go on, and therefore he called the ruler of this element Poseidon; the epsilon was probably inserted as an ornament.</div><div class="sentence"> Yet, perhaps, not so; but the name may have been originally written with a double lamda and not with a sigma, meaning that the God knew many things (Polla eidos).</div><div class="sentence"> And perhaps also he being the shaker of the earth, has been named from shaking (seiein), and then pi and delta have been added.</div><div class="sentence"> Pluto gives wealth (Ploutos), and his name means the giver of wealth, which comes out of the earth beneath.</div><div class="sentence"> People in general appear to imagine that the term Hades is connected with the invisible (aeides) and so they are led by their fears to call the God Pluto instead.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">326</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  And what is the true derivation?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">327</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In spite of the mistakes which are made about the power of this deity, and the foolish fears which people have of him, such as the fear of always being with him after death, and of the soul denuded of the body going to him (compare Rep.), my belief is that all is quite consistent, and that the office and name of the God really correspond.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">328</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, how is that?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">329</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will tell you my own opinion; but first, I should like to ask you which chain does any animal feel to be the stronger?</div><div class="sentence"> and which confines him more to the same spot,—desire or necessity?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">330</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Desire, Socrates, is stronger far.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">331</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you not think that many a one would escape from Hades, if he did not bind those who depart to him by the strongest of chains?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">332</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Assuredly they would.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">333</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if by the greatest of chains, then by some desire, as I should certainly infer, and not by necessity?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">334</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is clear.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">335</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And there are many desires?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">336</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">337</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And therefore by the greatest desire, if the chain is to be the greatest?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">338</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">339</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is any desire stronger than the thought that you will be made better by associating with another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">340</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">341</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not that the reason, Hermogenes, why no one, who has been to him, is willing to come back to us?</div><div class="sentence"> Even the Sirens, like all the rest of the world, have been laid under his spells.</div><div class="sentence"> Such a charm, as I imagine, is the God able to infuse into his words.</div><div class="sentence"> And, according to this view, he is the perfect and accomplished Sophist, and the great benefactor of the inhabitants of the other world; and even to us who are upon earth he sends from below exceeding blessings.</div><div class="sentence"> For he has much more than he wants down there; wherefore he is called Pluto (or the rich).</div><div class="sentence"> Note also, that he will have nothing to do with men while they are in the body, but only when the soul is liberated from the desires and evils of the body.</div><div class="sentence"> Now there is a great deal of philosophy and reflection in that; for in their liberated state he can bind them with the desire of virtue, but while they are flustered and maddened by the body, not even father Cronos himself would suffice to keep them with him in his own far-famed chains.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">342</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There is a deal of truth in what you say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">343</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Hermogenes, and the legislator called him Hades, not from the unseen (aeides)—far otherwise, but from his knowledge (eidenai) of all noble things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">344</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good; and what do we say of Demeter, and Here, and Apollo, and Athene, and Hephaestus, and Ares, and the other deities?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">345</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Demeter is e didousa meter, who gives food like a mother; Here is the lovely one (erate)—for Zeus, according to tradition, loved and married her; possibly also the name may have been given when the legislator was thinking of the heavens, and may be only a disguise of the air (aer), putting the end in the place of the beginning.</div><div class="sentence"> You will recognize the truth of this if you repeat the letters of Here several times over.</div><div class="sentence"> People dread the name of Pherephatta as they dread the name of Apollo,—and with as little reason; the fear, if I am not mistaken, only arises from their ignorance of the nature of names.</div><div class="sentence"> But they go changing the name into Phersephone, and they are terrified at this; whereas the new name means only that the Goddess is wise (sophe); for seeing that all things in the world are in motion (pheromenon), that principle which embraces and touches and is able to follow them, is wisdom.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore the Goddess may be truly called Pherepaphe (Pherepapha), or some name like it, because she touches that which is in motion (tou pheromenon ephaptomene), herein showing her wisdom.</div><div class="sentence"> And Hades, who is wise, consorts with her, because she is wise.</div><div class="sentence"> They alter her name into Pherephatta now-a-days, because the present generation care for euphony more than truth.</div><div class="sentence"> There is the other name, Apollo, which, as I was saying, is generally supposed to have some terrible signification.</div><div class="sentence"> Have you remarked this fact?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">346</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure I have, and what you say is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">347</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But the name, in my opinion, is really most expressive of the power of the God.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">348</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">349</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will endeavour to explain, for I do not believe that any single name could have been better adapted to express the attributes of the God, embracing and in a manner signifying all four of them,—music, and prophecy, and medicine, and archery.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">350</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That must be a strange name, and I should like to hear the explanation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">351</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Say rather an harmonious name, as beseems the God of Harmony.</div><div class="sentence"> In the first place, the purgations and purifications which doctors and diviners use, and their fumigations with drugs magical or medicinal, as well as their washings and lustral sprinklings, have all one and the same object, which is to make a man pure both in body and soul.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">352</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">353</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And is not Apollo the purifier, and the washer, and the absolver from all impurities?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">354</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">355</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then in reference to his ablutions and absolutions, as being the physician who orders them, he may be rightly called Apolouon (purifier); or in respect of his powers of divination, and his truth and sincerity, which is the same as truth, he may be most fitly called Aplos, from aplous (sincere), as in the Thessalian dialect, for all the Thessalians call him Aplos; also he is aei Ballon (always shooting), because he is a master archer who never misses; or again, the name may refer to his musical attributes, and then, as in akolouthos, and akoitis, and in many other words the alpha is supposed to mean “together,” so the meaning of the name Apollo will be “moving together,” whether in the poles of heaven as they are called, or in the harmony of song, which is termed concord, because he moves all together by an harmonious power, as astronomers and musicians ingeniously declare.</div><div class="sentence"> And he is the God who presides over harmony, and makes all things move together, both among Gods and among men.</div><div class="sentence"> And as in the words akolouthos and akoitis the alpha is substituted for an omicron, so the name Apollon is equivalent to omopolon; only the second lambda is added in order to avoid the ill-omened sound of destruction (apolon).</div><div class="sentence"> Now the suspicion of this destructive power still haunts the minds of some who do not consider the true value of the name, which, as I was saying just now, has reference to all the powers of the God, who is the single one, the everdarting, the purifier, the mover together (aplous, aei Ballon, apolouon, omopolon).</div><div class="sentence"> The name of the Muses and of music would seem to be derived from their making philosophical enquiries (mosthai); and Leto is called by this name, because she is such a gentle Goddess, and so willing (ethelemon) to grant our requests; or her name may be Letho, as she is often called by strangers—they seem to imply by it her amiability, and her smooth and easy-going way of behaving.</div><div class="sentence"> Artemis is named from her healthy (artemes), well-ordered nature, and because of her love of virginity, perhaps because she is a proficient in virtue (arete), and perhaps also as hating intercourse of the sexes (ton aroton misesasa).</div><div class="sentence"> He who gave the Goddess her name may have had any or all of these reasons.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">356</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What is the meaning of Dionysus and Aphrodite?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">357</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Son of Hipponicus, you ask a solemn question; there is a serious and also a facetious explanation of both these names; the serious explanation is not to be had from me, but there is no objection to your hearing the facetious one; for the Gods too love a joke.</div><div class="sentence"> Dionusos is simply didous oinon (giver of wine), Didoinusos, as he might be called in fun,—and oinos is properly oionous, because wine makes those who drink, think (oiesthai) that they have a mind (noun) when they have none.</div><div class="sentence"> The derivation of Aphrodite, born of the foam (aphros), may be fairly accepted on the authority of Hesiod.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">358</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Still there remains Athene, whom you, Socrates, as an Athenian, will surely not forget; there are also Hephaestus and Ares.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">359</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am not likely to forget them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">360</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">361</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is no difficulty in explaining the other appellation of Athene.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">362</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What other appellation?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">363</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We call her Pallas.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">364</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  To be sure.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">365</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And we cannot be wrong in supposing that this is derived from armed dances.</div><div class="sentence"> For the elevation of oneself or anything else above the earth, or by the use of the hands, we call shaking (pallein), or dancing.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">366</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">367</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then that is the explanation of the name Pallas?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">368</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; but what do you say of the other name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">369</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Athene?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">370</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">371</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is a graver matter, and there, my friend, the modern interpreters of Homer may, I think, assist in explaining the view of the ancients.</div><div class="sentence"> For most of these in their explanations of the poet, assert that he meant by Athene “mind” (nous) and “intelligence” (dianoia), and the maker of names appears to have had a singular notion about her; and indeed calls her by a still higher title, “divine intelligence” (Thou noesis), as though he would say:  This is she who has the mind of God (Theonoa);—using alpha as a dialectical variety for eta, and taking away iota and sigma (There seems to be some error in the MSS.</div><div class="sentence"> The meaning is that the word theonoa = theounoa is a curtailed form of theou noesis, but the omitted letters do not agree.).</div><div class="sentence"> Perhaps, however, the name Theonoe may mean “she who knows divine things” (Theia noousa) better than others.</div><div class="sentence"> Nor shall we be far wrong in supposing that the author of it wished to identify this Goddess with moral intelligence (en ethei noesin), and therefore gave her the name ethonoe; which, however, either he or his successors have altered into what they thought a nicer form, and called her Athene.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">372</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But what do you say of Hephaestus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">373</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Speak you of the princely lord of light (Phaeos istora)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">374</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Surely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">375</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Ephaistos is Phaistos, and has added the eta by attraction; that is obvious to anybody.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">376</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is very probable, until some more probable notion gets into your head.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">377</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  To prevent that, you had better ask what is the derivation of Ares.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">378</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What is Ares?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">379</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Ares may be called, if you will, from his manhood (arren) and manliness, or if you please, from his hard and unchangeable nature, which is the meaning of arratos:  the latter is a derivation in every way appropriate to the God of war.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">380</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">381</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And now, by the Gods, let us have no more of the Gods, for I am afraid of them; ask about anything but them, and thou shalt see how the steeds of Euthyphro can prance.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">382</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Only one more God!</div><div class="sentence"> I should like to know about Hermes, of whom I am said not to be a true son.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us make him out, and then I shall know whether there is any meaning in what Cratylus says.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">383</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I should imagine that the name Hermes has to do with speech, and signifies that he is the interpreter (ermeneus), or messenger, or thief, or liar, or bargainer; all that sort of thing has a great deal to do with language; as I was telling you, the word eirein is expressive of the use of speech, and there is an often-recurring Homeric word emesato, which means “he contrived”—out of these two words, eirein and mesasthai, the legislator formed the name of the God who invented language and speech; and we may imagine him dictating to us the use of this name:  “O my friends,” says he to us, “seeing that he is the contriver of tales or speeches, you may rightly call him Eirhemes.” And this has been improved by us, as we think, into Hermes.</div><div class="sentence"> Iris also appears to have been called from the verb “to tell” (eirein), because she was a messenger.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">384</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Then I am very sure that Cratylus was quite right in saying that I was no true son of Hermes (Ermogenes), for I am not a good hand at speeches.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">385</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is also reason, my friend, in Pan being the double-formed son of Hermes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">386</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How do you make that out?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">387</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You are aware that speech signifies all things (pan), and is always turning them round and round, and has two forms, true and false?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">388</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">389</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Is not the truth that is in him the smooth or sacred form which dwells above among the Gods, whereas falsehood dwells among men below, and is rough like the goat of tragedy; for tales and falsehoods have generally to do with the tragic or goatish life, and tragedy is the place of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">390</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">391</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then surely Pan, who is the declarer of all things (pan) and the perpetual mover (aei polon) of all things, is rightly called aipolos (goat-herd), he being the two-formed son of Hermes, smooth in his upper part, and rough and goatlike in his lower regions.</div><div class="sentence"> And, as the son of Hermes, he is speech or the brother of speech, and that brother should be like brother is no marvel.</div><div class="sentence"> But, as I was saying, my dear Hermogenes, let us get away from the Gods.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">392</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  From these sort of Gods, by all means, Socrates.</div><div class="sentence"> But why should we not discuss another kind of Gods—the sun, moon, stars, earth, aether, air, fire, water, the seasons, and the year?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">393</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You impose a great many tasks upon me.</div><div class="sentence"> Still, if you wish, I will not refuse.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">394</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  You will oblige me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">395</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How would you have me begin?</div><div class="sentence"> Shall I take first of all him whom you mentioned first—the sun?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">396</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">397</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The origin of the sun will probably be clearer in the Doric form, for the Dorians call him alios, and this name is given to him because when he rises he gathers (alizoi) men together or because he is always rolling in his course (aei eilein ion) about the earth; or from aiolein, of which the meaning is the same as poikillein (to variegate), because he variegates the productions of the earth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">398</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But what is selene (the moon)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">399</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That name is rather unfortunate for Anaxagoras.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">400</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">401</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The word seems to forestall his recent discovery, that the moon receives her light from the sun.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">402</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Why do you say so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">403</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The two words selas (brightness) and phos (light) have much the same meaning?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">404</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">405</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  This light about the moon is always new (neon) and always old (enon), if the disciples of Anaxagoras say truly.</div><div class="sentence"> For the sun in his revolution always adds new light, and there is the old light of the previous month.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">406</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">407</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The moon is not unfrequently called selanaia.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">408</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">409</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And as she has a light which is always old and always new (enon neon aei) she may very properly have the name selaenoneoaeia; and this when hammered into shape becomes selanaia.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">410</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  A real dithyrambic sort of name that, Socrates.</div><div class="sentence"> But what do you say of the month and the stars?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">411</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Meis (month) is called from meiousthai (to lessen), because suffering diminution; the name of astra (stars) seems to be derived from astrape, which is an improvement on anastrope, signifying the upsetting of the eyes (anastrephein opa).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">412</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you say of pur (fire) and udor (water)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">413</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am at a loss how to explain pur; either the muse of Euthyphro has deserted me, or there is some very great difficulty in the word.</div><div class="sentence"> Please, however, to note the contrivance which I adopt whenever I am in a difficulty of this sort.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">414</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What is it?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">415</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will tell you; but I should like to know first whether you can tell me what is the meaning of the pur?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">416</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed I cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">417</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall I tell you what I suspect to be the true explanation of this and several other words?—My belief is that they are of foreign origin.</div><div class="sentence"> For the Hellenes, especially those who were under the dominion of the barbarians, often borrowed from them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">418</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What is the inference?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">419</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, you know that any one who seeks to demonstrate the fitness of these names according to the Hellenic language, and not according to the language from which the words are derived, is rather likely to be at fault.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">420</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">421</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well then, consider whether this pur is not foreign; for the word is not easily brought into relation with the Hellenic tongue, and the Phrygians may be observed to have the same word slightly changed, just as they have udor (water) and kunes (dogs), and many other words.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">422</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">423</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Any violent interpretations of the words should be avoided; for something to say about them may easily be found.</div><div class="sentence"> And thus I get rid of pur and udor.</div><div class="sentence"> Aer (air), Hermogenes, may be explained as the element which raises (airei) things from the earth, or as ever flowing (aei rei), or because the flux of the air is wind, and the poets call the winds “air-blasts,” (aetai); he who uses the term may mean, so to speak, air-flux (aetorroun), in the sense of wind-flux (pneumatorroun); and because this moving wind may be expressed by either term he employs the word air (aer = aetes rheo).</div><div class="sentence"> Aither (aether) I should interpret as aeitheer; this may be correctly said, because this element is always running in a flux about the air (aei thei peri tou aera reon).</div><div class="sentence"> The meaning of the word ge (earth) comes out better when in the form of gaia, for the earth may be truly called “mother” (gaia, genneteira), as in the language of Homer (Od.)</div><div class="sentence"> gegaasi means gegennesthai.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">424</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">425</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What shall we take next?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">426</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There are orai (the seasons), and the two names of the year, eniautos and etos.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">427</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The orai should be spelt in the old Attic way, if you desire to know the probable truth about them; they are rightly called the orai because they divide (orizousin) the summers and winters and winds and the fruits of the earth.</div><div class="sentence"> The words eniautos and etos appear to be the same,—“that which brings to light the plants and growths of the earth in their turn, and passes them in review within itself (en eauto exetazei)”:  this is broken up into two words, eniautos from en eauto, and etos from etazei, just as the original name of Zeus was divided into Zena and Dia; and the whole proposition means that his power of reviewing from within is one, but has two names, two words etos and eniautos being thus formed out of a single proposition.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">428</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Indeed, Socrates, you make surprising progress.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">429</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am run away with.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">430</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">431</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But am not yet at my utmost speed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">432</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should like very much to know, in the next place, how you would explain the virtues.</div><div class="sentence"> What principle of correctness is there in those charming words—wisdom, understanding, justice, and the rest of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">433</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is a tremendous class of names which you are disinterring; still, as I have put on the lion’s skin, I must not be faint of heart; and I suppose that I must consider the meaning of wisdom (phronesis) and understanding (sunesis), and judgment (gnome), and knowledge (episteme), and all those other charming words, as you call them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">434</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Surely, we must not leave off until we find out their meaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">435</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  By the dog of Egypt I have a not bad notion which came into my head only this moment:  I believe that the primeval givers of names were undoubtedly like too many of our modern philosophers, who, in their search after the nature of things, are always getting dizzy from constantly going round and round, and then they imagine that the world is going round and round and moving in all directions; and this appearance, which arises out of their own internal condition, they suppose to be a reality of nature; they think that there is nothing stable or permanent, but only flux and motion, and that the world is always full of every sort of motion and change.</div><div class="sentence"> The consideration of the names which I mentioned has led me into making this reflection.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">436</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How is that, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">437</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Perhaps you did not observe that in the names which have been just cited, the motion or flux or generation of things is most surely indicated.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">438</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed, I never thought of it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">439</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Take the first of those which you mentioned; clearly that is a name indicative of motion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">440</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What was the name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">441</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Phronesis (wisdom), which may signify phoras kai rhou noesis (perception of motion and flux), or perhaps phoras onesis (the blessing of motion), but is at any rate connected with pheresthai (motion); gnome (judgment), again, certainly implies the ponderation or consideration (nomesis) of generation, for to ponder is the same as to consider; or, if you would rather, here is noesis, the very word just now mentioned, which is neou esis (the desire of the new); the word neos implies that the world is always in process of creation.</div><div class="sentence"> The giver of the name wanted to express this longing of the soul, for the original name was neoesis, and not noesis; but eta took the place of a double epsilon.</div><div class="sentence"> The word sophrosune is the salvation (soteria) of that wisdom (phronesis) which we were just now considering.</div><div class="sentence"> Epioteme (knowledge) is akin to this, and indicates that the soul which is good for anything follows (epetai) the motion of things, neither anticipating them nor falling behind them; wherefore the word should rather be read as epistemene, inserting epsilon nu.</div><div class="sentence"> Sunesis (understanding) may be regarded in like manner as a kind of conclusion; the word is derived from sunienai (to go along with), and, like epistasthai (to know), implies the progression of the soul in company with the nature of things.</div><div class="sentence"> Sophia (wisdom) is very dark, and appears not to be of native growth; the meaning is, touching the motion or stream of things.</div><div class="sentence"> You must remember that the poets, when they speak of the commencement of any rapid motion, often use the word esuthe (he rushed); and there was a famous Lacedaemonian who was named Sous (Rush), for by this word the Lacedaemonians signify rapid motion, and the touching (epaphe) of motion is expressed by sophia, for all things are supposed to be in motion.</div><div class="sentence"> Good (agathon) is the name which is given to the admirable (agasto) in nature; for, although all things move, still there are degrees of motion; some are swifter, some slower; but there are some things which are admirable for their swiftness, and this admirable part of nature is called agathon.</div><div class="sentence"> Dikaiosune (justice) is clearly dikaiou sunesis (understanding of the just); but the actual word dikaion is more difficult:  men are only agreed to a certain extent about justice, and then they begin to disagree.</div><div class="sentence"> For those who suppose all things to be in motion conceive the greater part of nature to be a mere receptacle;
