and they say that there is a penetrating power which passes through all this, and is the instrument of creation in all, and is the subtlest and swiftest element; for if it were not the subtlest, and a power which none can keep out, and also the swiftest, passing by other things as if they were standing still, it could not penetrate through the moving universe.</div><div class="sentence"> And this element, which superintends all things and pierces (diaion) all, is rightly called dikaion; the letter k is only added for the sake of euphony.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus far, as I was saying, there is a general agreement about the nature of justice; but I, Hermogenes, being an enthusiastic disciple, have been told in a mystery that the justice of which I am speaking is also the cause of the world:  now a cause is that because of which anything is created; and some one comes and whispers in my ear that justice is rightly so called because partaking of the nature of the cause, and I begin, after hearing what he has said, to interrogate him gently:  “Well, my excellent friend,” say I, “but if all this be true, I still want to know what is justice.” Thereupon they think that I ask tiresome questions, and am leaping over the barriers, and have been already sufficiently answered, and they try to satisfy me with one derivation after another, and at length they quarrel.</div><div class="sentence"> For one of them says that justice is the sun, and that he only is the piercing (diaionta) and burning (kaonta) element which is the guardian of nature.</div><div class="sentence"> And when I joyfully repeat this beautiful notion, I am answered by the satirical remark, “What, is there no justice in the world when the sun is down?” And when I earnestly beg my questioner to tell me his own honest opinion, he says, “Fire in the abstract”; but this is not very intelligible.</div><div class="sentence"> Another says, “No, not fire in the abstract, but the abstraction of heat in the fire.” Another man professes to laugh at all this, and says, as Anaxagoras says, that justice is mind, for mind, as they say, has absolute power, and mixes with nothing, and orders all things, and passes through all things.</div><div class="sentence"> At last, my friend, I find myself in far greater perplexity about the nature of justice than I was before I began to learn.</div><div class="sentence"> But still I am of opinion that the name, which has led me into this digression, was given to justice for the reasons which I have mentioned.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">442</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think, Socrates, that you are not improvising now; you must have heard this from some one else.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">443</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And not the rest?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">444</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Hardly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">445</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, let me go on in the hope of making you believe in the originality of the rest.</div><div class="sentence"> What remains after justice?</div><div class="sentence"> I do not think that we have as yet discussed courage (andreia),—injustice (adikia), which is obviously nothing more than a hindrance to the penetrating principle (diaiontos), need not be considered.</div><div class="sentence"> Well, then, the name of andreia seems to imply a battle;—this battle is in the world of existence, and according to the doctrine of flux is only the counterflux (enantia rhon):  if you extract the delta from andreia, the name at once signifies the thing, and you may clearly understand that andreia is not the stream opposed to every stream, but only to that which is contrary to justice, for otherwise courage would not have been praised.</div><div class="sentence"> The words arren (male) and aner (man) also contain a similar allusion to the same principle of the upward flux (te ano rhon).</div><div class="sentence"> Gune (woman) I suspect to be the same word as goun (birth):  thelu (female) appears to be partly derived from thele (the teat), because the teat is like rain, and makes things flourish (tethelenai).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">446</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is surely probable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">447</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; and the very word thallein (to flourish) seems to figure the growth of youth, which is swift and sudden ever.</div><div class="sentence"> And this is expressed by the legislator in the name, which is a compound of thein (running), and allesthai (leaping).</div><div class="sentence"> Pray observe how I gallop away when I get on smooth ground.</div><div class="sentence"> There are a good many names generally thought to be of importance, which have still to be explained.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">448</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">449</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is the meaning of the word techne (art), for example.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">450</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">451</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That may be identified with echonoe, and expresses the possession of mind:  you have only to take away the tau and insert two omichrons, one between the chi and nu, and another between the nu and eta.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">452</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is a very shabby etymology.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">453</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my dear friend; but then you know that the original names have been long ago buried and disguised by people sticking on and stripping off letters for the sake of euphony, and twisting and bedizening them in all sorts of ways:  and time too may have had a share in the change.</div><div class="sentence"> Take, for example, the word katoptron; why is the letter rho inserted?</div><div class="sentence"> This must surely be the addition of some one who cares nothing about the truth, but thinks only of putting the mouth into shape.</div><div class="sentence"> And the additions are often such that at last no human being can possibly make out the original meaning of the word.</div><div class="sentence"> Another example is the word sphigx, sphiggos, which ought properly to be phigx, phiggos, and there are other examples.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">454</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is quite true, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">455</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet, if you are permitted to put in and pull out any letters which you please, names will be too easily made, and any name may be adapted to any object.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">456</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">457</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, that is true.</div><div class="sentence"> And therefore a wise dictator, like yourself, should observe the laws of moderation and probability.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">458</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Such is my desire.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">459</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And mine, too, Hermogenes.</div><div class="sentence"> But do not be too much of a precisian, or “you will unnerve me of my strength (Iliad.</div><div class="sentence"> ).” When you have allowed me to add mechane (contrivance) to techne (art) I shall be at the top of my bent, for I conceive mechane to be a sign of great accomplishment—anein; for mekos has the meaning of greatness, and these two, mekos and anein, make up the word mechane.</div><div class="sentence"> But, as I was saying, being now at the top of my bent, I should like to consider the meaning of the two words arete (virtue) and kakia (vice); arete I do not as yet understand, but kakia is transparent, and agrees with the principles which preceded, for all things being in a flux (ionton), kakia is kakos ion (going badly); and this evil motion when existing in the soul has the general name of kakia, or vice, specially appropriated to it.</div><div class="sentence"> The meaning of kakos ienai may be further illustrated by the use of deilia (cowardice), which ought to have come after andreia, but was forgotten, and, as I fear, is not the only word which has been passed over.</div><div class="sentence"> Deilia signifies that the soul is bound with a strong chain (desmos), for lian means strength, and therefore deilia expresses the greatest and strongest bond of the soul; and aporia (difficulty) is an evil of the same nature (from a (alpha) not, and poreuesthai to go), like anything else which is an impediment to motion and movement.</div><div class="sentence"> Then the word kakia appears to mean kakos ienai, or going badly, or limping and halting; of which the consequence is, that the soul becomes filled with vice.</div><div class="sentence"> And if kakia is the name of this sort of thing, arete will be the opposite of it, signifying in the first place ease of motion, then that the stream of the good soul is unimpeded, and has therefore the attribute of ever flowing without let or hindrance, and is therefore called arete, or, more correctly, aeireite (ever-flowing), and may perhaps have had another form, airete (eligible), indicating that nothing is more eligible than virtue, and this has been hammered into arete.</div><div class="sentence"> I daresay that you will deem this to be another invention of mine, but I think that if the previous word kakia was right, then arete is also right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">460</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But what is the meaning of kakon, which has played so great a part in your previous discourse?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">461</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is a very singular word about which I can hardly form an opinion, and therefore I must have recourse to my ingenious device.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">462</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What device?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">463</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The device of a foreign origin, which I shall give to this word also.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">464</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very likely you are right; but suppose that we leave these words and endeavour to see the rationale of kalon and aischron.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">465</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The meaning of aischron is evident, being only aei ischon roes (always preventing from flowing), and this is in accordance with our former derivations.</div><div class="sentence"> For the name-giver was a great enemy to stagnation of all sorts, and hence he gave the name aeischoroun to that which hindered the flux (aei ischon roun), and that is now beaten together into aischron.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">466</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But what do you say of kalon?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">467</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is more obscure; yet the form is only due to the quantity, and has been changed by altering omicron upsilon into omicron.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">468</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">469</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  This name appears to denote mind.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">470</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">471</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me ask you what is the cause why anything has a name; is not the principle which imposes the name the cause?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">472</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">473</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And must not this be the mind of Gods, or of men, or of both?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">474</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">475</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Is not mind that which called (kalesan) things by their names, and is not mind the beautiful (kalon)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">476</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is evident.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">477</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are not the works of intelligence and mind worthy of praise, and are not other works worthy of blame?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">478</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">479</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Physic does the work of a physician, and carpentering does the works of a carpenter?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">480</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Exactly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">481</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the principle of beauty does the works of beauty?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">482</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">483</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that principle we affirm to be mind?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">484</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">485</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then mind is rightly called beauty because she does the works which we recognize and speak of as the beautiful?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">486</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is evident.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">487</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What more names remain to us?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">488</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There are the words which are connected with agathon and kalon, such as sumpheron and lusiteloun, ophelimon, kerdaleon, and their opposites.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">489</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The meaning of sumpheron (expedient) I think that you may discover for yourself by the light of the previous examples,—for it is a sister word to episteme, meaning just the motion (pora) of the soul accompanying the world, and things which are done upon this principle are called sumphora or sumpheronta, because they are carried round with the world.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">490</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is probable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">491</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, cherdaleon (gainful) is called from cherdos (gain), but you must alter the delta into nu if you want to get at the meaning; for this word also signifies good, but in another way; he who gave the name intended to express the power of admixture (kerannumenon) and universal penetration in the good; in forming the word, however, he inserted a delta instead of a nu, and so made kerdos.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">492</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but what is lusiteloun (profitable)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">493</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose, Hermogenes, that people do not mean by the profitable the gainful or that which pays (luei) the retailer, but they use the word in the sense of swift.</div><div class="sentence"> You regard the profitable (lusiteloun), as that which being the swiftest thing in existence, allows of no stay in things and no pause or end of motion, but always, if there begins to be any end, lets things go again (luei), and makes motion immortal and unceasing:  and in this point of view, as appears to me, the good is happily denominated lusiteloun—being that which looses (luon) the end (telos) of motion.</div><div class="sentence"> Ophelimon (the advantageous) is derived from ophellein, meaning that which creates and increases; this latter is a common Homeric word, and has a foreign character.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">494</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  And what do you say of their opposites?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">495</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Of such as are mere negatives I hardly think that I need speak.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">496</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Which are they?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">497</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The words axumphoron (inexpedient), anopheles (unprofitable), alusiteles (unadvantageous), akerdes (ungainful).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">498</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">499</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I would rather take the words blaberon (harmful), zemiodes (hurtful).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">500</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">501</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The word blaberon is that which is said to hinder or harm (blaptein) the stream (roun); blapton is boulomenon aptein (seeking to hold or bind); for aptein is the same as dein, and dein is always a term of censure; boulomenon aptein roun (wanting to bind the stream) would properly be boulapteroun, and this, as I imagine, is improved into blaberon.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">502</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  You bring out curious results, Socrates, in the use of names; and when I hear the word boulapteroun I cannot help imagining that you are making your mouth into a flute, and puffing away at some prelude to Athene.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">503</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is the fault of the makers of the name, Hermogenes; not mine.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">504</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true; but what is the derivation of zemiodes?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">505</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What is the meaning of zemiodes?—let me remark, Hermogenes, how right I was in saying that great changes are made in the meaning of words by putting in and pulling out letters; even a very slight permutation will sometimes give an entirely opposite sense; I may instance the word deon, which occurs to me at the moment, and reminds me of what I was going to say to you, that the fine fashionable language of modern times has twisted and disguised and entirely altered the original meaning both of deon, and also of zemiodes, which in the old language is clearly indicated.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">506</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">507</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I will try to explain.</div><div class="sentence"> You are aware that our forefathers loved the sounds iota and delta, especially the women, who are most conservative of the ancient language, but now they change iota into eta or epsilon, and delta into zeta; this is supposed to increase the grandeur of the sound.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">508</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  How do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">509</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  For example, in very ancient times they called the day either imera or emera (short e), which is called by us emera (long e).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">510</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">511</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you observe that only the ancient form shows the intention of the giver of the name?</div><div class="sentence"> of which the reason is, that men long for (imeirousi) and love the light which comes after the darkness, and is therefore called imera, from imeros, desire.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">512</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">513</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But now the name is so travestied that you cannot tell the meaning, although there are some who imagine the day to be called emera because it makes things gentle (emera different accents).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">514</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Such is my view.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">515</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And do you know that the ancients said duogon and not zugon?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">516</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  They did so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">517</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And zugon (yoke) has no meaning,—it ought to be duogon, which word expresses the binding of two together (duein agoge) for the purpose of drawing;—this has been changed into zugon, and there are many other examples of similar changes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">518</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There are.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">519</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Proceeding in the same train of thought I may remark that the word deon (obligation) has a meaning which is the opposite of all the other appellations of good; for deon is here a species of good, and is, nevertheless, the chain (desmos) or hinderer of motion, and therefore own brother of blaberon.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">520</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates; that is quite plain.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">521</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Not if you restore the ancient form, which is more likely to be the correct one, and read dion instead of deon; if you convert the epsilon into an iota after the old fashion, this word will then agree with other words meaning good; for dion, not deon, signifies the good, and is a term of praise; and the author of names has not contradicted himself, but in all these various appellations, deon (obligatory), ophelimon (advantageous), lusiteloun (profitable), kerdaleon (gainful), agathon (good), sumpheron (expedient), euporon (plenteous), the same conception is implied of the ordering or all-pervading principle which is praised, and the restraining and binding principle which is censured.</div><div class="sentence"> And this is further illustrated by the word zemiodes (hurtful), which if the zeta is only changed into delta as in the ancient language, becomes demiodes; and this name, as you will perceive, is given to that which binds motion (dounti ion).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">522</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you say of edone (pleasure), lupe (pain), epithumia (desire), and the like, Socrates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">523</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I do not think, Hermogenes, that there is any great difficulty about them—edone is e (eta) onesis, the action which tends to advantage; and the original form may be supposed to have been eone, but this has been altered by the insertion of the delta.</div><div class="sentence"> Lupe appears to be derived from the relaxation (luein) which the body feels when in sorrow; ania (trouble) is the hindrance of motion (alpha and ienai); algedon (distress), if I am not mistaken, is a foreign word, which is derived from aleinos (grievous); odune (grief) is called from the putting on (endusis) sorrow; in achthedon (vexation) “the word too labours,” as any one may see; chara (joy) is the very expression of the fluency and diffusion of the soul (cheo); terpsis (delight) is so called from the pleasure creeping (erpon) through the soul, which may be likened to a breath (pnoe) and is properly erpnoun, but has been altered by time into terpnon; eupherosune (cheerfulness) and epithumia explain themselves; the former, which ought to be eupherosune and has been changed euphrosune, is named, as every one may see, from the soul moving (pheresthai) in harmony with nature; epithumia is really e epi ton thumon iousa dunamis, the power which enters into the soul; thumos (passion) is called from the rushing (thuseos) and boiling of the soul; imeros (desire) denotes the stream (rous) which most draws the soul dia ten esin tes roes—because flowing with desire (iemenos), and expresses a longing after things and violent attraction of the soul to them, and is termed imeros from possessing this power; pothos (longing) is expressive of the desire of that which is not present but absent, and in another place (pou); this is the reason why the name pothos is applied to things absent, as imeros is to things present; eros (love) is so called because flowing in (esron) from without; the stream is not inherent, but is an influence introduced through the eyes, and from flowing in was called esros (influx) in the old time when they used omicron for omega, and is called eros, now that omega is substituted for omicron.</div><div class="sentence"> But why do you not give me another word?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">524</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What do you think of doxa (opinion), and that class of words?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">525</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Doxa is either derived from dioxis (pursuit), and expresses the march of the soul in the pursuit of knowledge, or from the shooting of a bow (toxon); the latter is more likely, and is confirmed by oiesis (thinking), which is only oisis (moving), and implies the movement of the soul to the essential nature of each thing—just as boule (counsel) has to do with shooting (bole); and boulesthai (to wish) combines the notion of aiming and deliberating—all these words seem to follow doxa, and all involve the idea of shooting, just as aboulia, absence of counsel, on the other hand, is a mishap, or missing, or mistaking of the mark, or aim, or proposal, or object.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">526</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  You are quickening your pace now, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">527</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why yes, the end I now dedicate to God, not, however, until I have explained anagke (necessity), which ought to come next, and ekousion (the voluntary).</div><div class="sentence"> Ekousion is certainly the yielding (eikon) and unresisting—the notion implied is yielding and not opposing, yielding, as I was just now saying, to that motion which is in accordance with our will; but the necessary and resistant being contrary to our will, implies error and ignorance; the idea is taken from walking through a ravine which is impassable, and rugged, and overgrown, and impedes motion—and this is the derivation of the word anagkaion (necessary) an agke ion, going through a ravine.</div><div class="sentence"> But while my strength lasts let us persevere, and I hope that you will persevere with your questions.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">528</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, then, let me ask about the greatest and noblest, such as aletheia (truth) and pseudos (falsehood) and on (being), not forgetting to enquire why the word onoma (name), which is the theme of our discussion, has this name of onoma.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">529</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You know the word maiesthai (to seek)?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">530</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes;—meaning the same as zetein (to enquire).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">531</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The word onoma seems to be a compressed sentence, signifying on ou zetema (being for which there is a search); as is still more obvious in onomaston (notable), which states in so many words that real existence is that for which there is a seeking (on ou masma); aletheia is also an agglomeration of theia ale (divine wandering), implying the divine motion of existence; pseudos (falsehood) is the opposite of motion; here is another ill name given by the legislator to stagnation and forced inaction, which he compares to sleep (eudein); but the original meaning of the word is disguised by the addition of psi; on and ousia are ion with an iota broken off; this agrees with the true principle, for being (on) is also moving (ion), and the same may be said of not being, which is likewise called not going (oukion or ouki on = ouk ion).</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">532</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  You have hammered away at them manfully; but suppose that some one were to say to you, what is the word ion, and what are reon and doun?—show me their fitness.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">533</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  You mean to say, how should I answer him?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">534</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">535</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  One way of giving the appearance of an answer has been already suggested.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">536</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  What way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">537</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  To say that names which we do not understand are of foreign origin; and this is very likely the right answer, and something of this kind may be true of them; but also the original forms of words may have been lost in the lapse of ages; names have been so twisted in all manner of ways, that I should not be surprised if the old language when compared with that now in use would appear to us to be a barbarous tongue.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">538</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very likely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">539</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, very likely.</div><div class="sentence"> But still the enquiry demands our earnest attention and we must not flinch.</div><div class="sentence"> For we should remember, that if a person go on analysing names into words, and enquiring also into the elements out of which the words are formed, and keeps on always repeating this process, he who has to answer him must at last give up the enquiry in despair.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">540</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">541</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And at what point ought he to lose heart and give up the enquiry?</div><div class="sentence"> Must he not stop when he comes to the names which are the elements of all other names and sentences; for these cannot be supposed to be made up of other names?</div><div class="sentence"> The word agathon (good), for example, is, as we were saying, a compound of agastos (admirable) and thoos (swift).</div><div class="sentence"> And probably thoos is made up of other elements, and these again of others.</div><div class="sentence"> But if we take a word which is incapable of further resolution, then we shall be right in saying that we have at last reached a primary element, which need not be resolved any further.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">542</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I believe you to be in the right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">543</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And suppose the names about which you are now asking should turn out to be primary elements, must not their truth or law be examined according to some new method?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">544</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very likely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">545</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite so, Hermogenes; all that has preceded would lead to this conclusion.</div><div class="sentence"> And if, as I think, the conclusion is true, then I shall again say to you, come and help me, that I may not fall into some absurdity in stating the principle of primary names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">546</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me hear, and I will do my best to assist you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">547</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I think that you will acknowledge with me, that one principle is applicable to all names, primary as well as secondary—when they are regarded simply as names, there is no difference in them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">548</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">549</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  All the names that we have been explaining were intended to indicate the nature of things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">550</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Of course.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">551</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that this is true of the primary quite as much as of the secondary names, is implied in their being names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">552</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Surely.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">553</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But the secondary, as I conceive, derive their significance from the primary.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">554</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That is evident.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">555</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good; but then how do the primary names which precede analysis show the natures of things, as far as they can be shown; which they must do, if they are to be real names?</div><div class="sentence"> And here I will ask you a question:  Suppose that we had no voice or tongue, and wanted to communicate with one another, should we not, like the deaf and dumb, make signs with the hands and head and the rest of the body?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">556</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There would be no choice, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">557</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We should imitate the nature of the thing; the elevation of our hands to heaven would mean lightness and upwardness; heaviness and downwardness would be expressed by letting them drop to the ground; if we were describing the running of a horse, or any other animal, we should make our bodies and their gestures as like as we could to them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">558</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I do not see that we could do anything else.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">559</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  We could not; for by bodily imitation only can the body ever express anything.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">560</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">561</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And when we want to express ourselves, either with the voice, or tongue, or mouth, the expression is simply their imitation of that which we want to express.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">562</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  It must be so, I think.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">563</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then a name is a vocal imitation of that which the vocal imitator names or imitates?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">564</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">565</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, my friend, I am disposed to think that we have not reached the truth as yet.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">566</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Why not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">567</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Because if we have we shall be obliged to admit that the people who imitate sheep, or cocks, or other animals, name that which they imitate.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">568</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">569</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then could I have been right in what I was saying?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">570</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  In my opinion, no.</div><div class="sentence"> But I wish that you would tell me, Socrates, what sort of an imitation is a name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">571</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, I should reply, not a musical imitation, although that is also vocal; nor, again, an imitation of what music imitates; these, in my judgment, would not be naming.</div><div class="sentence"> Let me put the matter as follows:  All objects have sound and figure, and many have colour?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">572</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">573</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But the art of naming appears not to be concerned with imitations of this kind; the arts which have to do with them are music and drawing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">574</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">575</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Again, is there not an essence of each thing, just as there is a colour, or sound?</div><div class="sentence"> And is there not an essence of colour and sound as well as of anything else which may be said to have an essence?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">576</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I should think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">577</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, and if any one could express the essence of each thing in letters and syllables, would he not express the nature of each thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">578</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Quite so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">579</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The musician and the painter were the two names which you gave to the two other imitators.</div><div class="sentence"> What will this imitator be called?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">580</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I imagine, Socrates, that he must be the namer, or name-giver, of whom we are in search.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">581</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  If this is true, then I think that we are in a condition to consider the names ron (stream), ienai (to go), schesis (retention), about which you were asking; and we may see whether the namer has grasped the nature of them in letters and syllables in such a manner as to imitate the essence or not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">582</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">583</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But are these the only primary names, or are there others?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">584</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  There must be others.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">585</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  So I should expect.</div><div class="sentence"> But how shall we further analyse them, and where does the imitator begin?</div><div class="sentence"> Imitation of the essence is made by syllables and letters; ought we not, therefore, first to separate the letters, just as those who are beginning rhythm first distinguish the powers of elementary, and then of compound sounds, and when they have done so, but not before, they proceed to the consideration of rhythms?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">586</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">587</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Must we not begin in the same way with letters; first separating the vowels, and then the consonants and mutes (letters which are neither vowels nor semivowels), into classes, according to the received distinctions of the learned; also the semivowels, which are neither vowels, nor yet mutes; and distinguishing into classes the vowels themselves?</div><div class="sentence"> And when we have perfected the classification of things, we shall give them names, and see whether, as in the case of letters, there are any classes to which they may be all referred (cf.</div><div class="sentence"> Phaedrus); and hence we shall see their natures, and see, too, whether they have in them classes as there are in the letters; and when we have well considered all this, we shall know how to apply them to what they resemble—whether one letter is used to denote one thing, or whether there is to be an admixture of several of them; just, as in painting, the painter who wants to depict anything sometimes uses purple only, or any other colour, and sometimes mixes up several colours, as his method is when he has to paint flesh colour or anything of that kind—he uses his colours as his figures appear to require them; and so, too, we shall apply letters to the expression of objects, either single letters when required, or several letters; and so we shall form syllables, as they are called, and from syllables make nouns and verbs; and thus, at last, from the combinations of nouns and verbs arrive at language, large and fair and whole; and as the painter made a figure, even so shall we make speech by the art of the namer or the rhetorician, or by some other art.</div><div class="sentence"> Not that I am literally speaking of ourselves, but I was carried away—meaning to say that this was the way in which (not we but) the ancients formed language, and what they put together we must take to pieces in like manner, if we are to attain a scientific view of the whole subject, and we must see whether the primary, and also whether the secondary elements are rightly given or not, for if they are not, the composition of them, my dear Hermogenes, will be a sorry piece of work, and in the wrong direction.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">588</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  That, Socrates, I can quite believe.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">589</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but do you suppose that you will be able to analyse them in this way?</div><div class="sentence"> for I am certain that I should not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">590</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Much less am I likely to be able.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">591</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Shall we leave them, then?</div><div class="sentence"> or shall we seek to discover, if we can, something about them, according to the measure of our ability, saying by way of preface, as I said before of the Gods, that of the truth about them we know nothing, and do but entertain human notions of them.</div><div class="sentence"> And in this present enquiry, let us say to ourselves, before we proceed, that the higher method is the one which we or others who would analyse language to any good purpose must follow; but under the circumstances, as men say, we must do as well as we can.</div><div class="sentence"> What do you think?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">592</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  I very much approve.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">593</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That objects should be imitated in letters and syllables, and so find expression, may appear ridiculous, Hermogenes, but it cannot be avoided—there is no better principle to which we can look for the truth of first names.</div><div class="sentence"> Deprived of this, we must have recourse to divine help, like the tragic poets, who in any perplexity have their gods waiting in the air; and must get out of our difficulty in like fashion, by saying that “the Gods gave the first names, and therefore they are right.” This will be the best contrivance, or perhaps that other notion may be even better still, of deriving them from some barbarous people, for the barbarians are older than we are; or we may say that antiquity has cast a veil over them, which is the same sort of excuse as the last; for all these are not reasons but only ingenious excuses for having no reasons concerning the truth of words.</div><div class="sentence"> And yet any sort of ignorance of first or primitive names involves an ignorance of secondary words; for they can only be explained by the primary.</div><div class="sentence"> Clearly then the professor of languages should be able to give a very lucid explanation of first names, or let him be assured he will only talk nonsense about the rest.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you not suppose this to be true?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">594</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">595</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  My first notions of original names are truly wild and ridiculous, though I have no objection to impart them to you if you desire, and I hope that you will communicate to me in return anything better which you may have.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">596</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  Fear not; I will do my best.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">597</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In the first place, the letter rho appears to me to be the general instrument expressing all motion (kinesis).</div><div class="sentence"> But I have not yet explained the meaning of this latter word, which is just iesis (going); for the letter eta was not in use among the ancients, who only employed epsilon; and the root is kiein, which is a foreign form, the same as ienai.</div><div class="sentence"> And the old word kinesis will be correctly given as iesis in corresponding modern letters.</div><div class="sentence"> Assuming this foreign root kiein, and allowing for the change of the eta and the insertion of the nu, we have kinesis, which should have been kieinsis or eisis; and stasis is the negative of ienai (or eisis), and has been improved into stasis.</div><div class="sentence"> Now the letter rho, as I was saying, appeared to the imposer of names an excellent instrument for the expression of motion; and he frequently uses the letter for this purpose:  for example, in the actual words rein and roe he represents motion by rho; also in the words tromos (trembling), trachus (rugged); and again, in words such as krouein (strike), thrauein (crush), ereikein (bruise), thruptein (break), kermatixein (crumble), rumbein (whirl):  of all these sorts of movements he generally finds an expression in the letter R, because, as I imagine, he had observed that the tongue was most agitated and least at rest in the pronunciation of this letter, which he therefore used in order to express motion, just as by the letter iota he expresses the subtle elements which pass through all things.</div><div class="sentence"> This is why he uses the letter iota as imitative of motion, ienai, iesthai.</div><div class="sentence"> And there is another class of letters, phi, psi, sigma, and xi, of which the pronunciation is accompanied by great expenditure of breath; these are used in the imitation of such notions as psuchron (shivering), xeon (seething), seiesthai, (to be shaken), seismos (shock), and are always introduced by the giver of names when he wants to imitate what is phusodes (windy).</div><div class="sentence"> He seems to have thought that the closing and pressure of the tongue in the utterance of delta and tau was expressive of binding and rest in a place:  he further observed the liquid movement of lambda, in the pronunciation of which the tongue slips, and in this he found the expression of smoothness, as in leios (level), and in the word oliothanein (to slip) itself, liparon (sleek), in the word kollodes (gluey), and the like:  the heavier sound of gamma detained the slipping tongue, and the union of the two gave the notion of a glutinous clammy nature, as in glischros, glukus, gloiodes.</div><div class="sentence"> The nu he observed to be sounded from within, and therefore to have a notion of inwardness; hence he introduced the sound in endos and entos:  alpha he assigned to the expression of size, and nu of length, because they are great letters:  omicron was the sign of roundness, and therefore there is plenty of omicron mixed up in the word goggulon (round).</div><div class="sentence"> Thus did the legislator, reducing all things into letters and syllables, and impressing on them names and signs, and out of them by imitation compounding other signs.</div><div class="sentence"> That is my view, Hermogenes, of the truth of names; but I should like to hear what Cratylus has more to say.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">598</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  But, Socrates, as I was telling you before, Cratylus mystifies me; he says that there is a fitness of names, but he never explains what is this fitness, so that I cannot tell whether his obscurity is intended or not.</div><div class="sentence"> Tell me now, Cratylus, here in the presence of Socrates, do you agree in what Socrates has been saying about names, or have you something better of your own?</div><div class="sentence"> and if you have, tell me what your view is, and then you will either learn of Socrates, or Socrates and I will learn of you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">599</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but surely, Hermogenes, you do not suppose that you can learn, or I explain, any subject of importance all in a moment; at any rate, not such a subject as language, which is, perhaps, the very greatest of all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">600</span><div class="speaker">HERMOGENES</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed; but, as Hesiod says, and I agree with him, “to add little to little” is worth while.</div><div class="sentence"> And, therefore, if you think that you can add anything at all, however small, to our knowledge, take a little trouble and oblige Socrates, and me too, who certainly have a claim upon you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">601</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I am by no means positive, Cratylus, in the view which Hermogenes and myself have worked out; and therefore do not hesitate to say what you think, which if it be better than my own view I shall gladly accept.</div><div class="sentence"> And I should not be at all surprized to find that you have found some better notion.</div><div class="sentence"> For you have evidently reflected on these matters and have had teachers, and if you have really a better theory of the truth of names, you may count me in the number of your disciples.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">602</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  You are right, Socrates, in saying that I have made a study of these matters, and I might possibly convert you into a disciple.</div><div class="sentence"> But I fear that the opposite is more probable, and I already find myself moved to say to you what Achilles in the “Prayers” says to Ajax,—</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">603</span> <div class="sentence">  “Illustrious Ajax, son of Telamon, lord of the people, You appear to have spoken in all things much to my mind.”</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">604</span> <div class="sentence">  And you, Socrates, appear to me to be an oracle, and to give answers much to my mind, whether you are inspired by Euthyphro, or whether some Muse may have long been an inhabitant of your breast, unconsciously to yourself.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">605</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Excellent Cratylus, I have long been wondering at my own wisdom; I cannot trust myself.</div><div class="sentence"> And I think that I ought to stop and ask myself What am I saying?</div><div class="sentence"> for there is nothing worse than self-deception—when the deceiver is always at home and always with you—it is quite terrible, and therefore I ought often to retrace my steps and endeavour to “look fore and aft,” in the words of the aforesaid Homer.</div><div class="sentence"> And now let me see; where are we?</div><div class="sentence"> Have we not been saying that the correct name indicates the nature of the thing: —has this proposition been sufficiently proven?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">606</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, what you say, as I am disposed to think, is quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">607</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Names, then, are given in order to instruct?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">608</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">609</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And naming is an art, and has artificers?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">610</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">611</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And who are they?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">612</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  The legislators, of whom you spoke at first.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">613</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And does this art grow up among men like other arts?</div><div class="sentence"> Let me explain what I mean:  of painters, some are better and some worse?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">614</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">615</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  The better painters execute their works, I mean their figures, better, and the worse execute them worse; and of builders also, the better sort build fairer houses, and the worse build them worse.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">616</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">617</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And among legislators, there are some who do their work better and some worse?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">618</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  No; there I do not agree with you.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">619</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you do not think that some laws are better and others worse?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">620</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  No, indeed.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">621</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Or that one name is better than another?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">622</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">623</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then all names are rightly imposed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">624</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, if they are names at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">625</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, what do you say to the name of our friend Hermogenes, which was mentioned before: —assuming that he has nothing of the nature of Hermes in him, shall we say that this is a wrong name, or not his name at all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">626</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should reply that Hermogenes is not his name at all, but only appears to be his, and is really the name of somebody else, who has the nature which corresponds to it.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">627</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if a man were to call him Hermogenes, would he not be even speaking falsely?</div><div class="sentence"> For there may be a doubt whether you can call him Hermogenes, if he is not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">628</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  What do you mean?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">629</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Are you maintaining that falsehood is impossible?</div><div class="sentence"> For if this is your meaning I should answer, that there have been plenty of liars in all ages.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">630</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Why, Socrates, how can a man say that which is not?—say something and yet say nothing?</div><div class="sentence"> For is not falsehood saying the thing which is not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">631</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Your argument, friend, is too subtle for a man of my age.</div><div class="sentence"> But I should like to know whether you are one of those philosophers who think that falsehood may be spoken but not said?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">632</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Neither spoken nor said.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">633</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nor uttered nor addressed?</div><div class="sentence"> For example:  If a person, saluting you in a foreign country, were to take your hand and say:  “Hail, Athenian stranger, Hermogenes, son of Smicrion”—these words, whether spoken, said, uttered, or addressed, would have no application to you but only to our friend Hermogenes, or perhaps to nobody at all?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">634</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  In my opinion, Socrates, the speaker would only be talking nonsense.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">635</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but that will be quite enough for me, if you will tell me whether the nonsense would be true or false, or partly true and partly false: —which is all that I want to know.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">636</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that he would be putting himself in motion to no purpose; and that his words would be an unmeaning sound like the noise of hammering at a brazen pot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">637</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But let us see, Cratylus, whether we cannot find a meeting-point, for you would admit that the name is not the same with the thing named?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">638</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">639</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you further acknowledge that the name is an imitation of the thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">640</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">641</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And you would say that pictures are also imitations of things, but in another way?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">642</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">643</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I believe you may be right, but I do not rightly understand you.</div><div class="sentence"> Please to say, then, whether both sorts of imitation (I mean both pictures or words) are not equally attributable and applicable to the things of which they are the imitation.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">644</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  They are.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">645</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  First look at the matter thus:  you may attribute the likeness of the man to the man, and of the woman to the woman; and so on?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">646</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">647</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And conversely you may attribute the likeness of the man to the woman, and of the woman to the man?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">648</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">649</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And are both modes of assigning them right, or only the first?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">650</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Only the first.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">651</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is to say, the mode of assignment which attributes to each that which belongs to them and is like them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">652</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is my view.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">653</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Now then, as I am desirous that we being friends should have a good understanding about the argument, let me state my view to you:  the first mode of assignment, whether applied to figures or to names, I call right, and when applied to names only, true as well as right; and the other mode of giving and assigning the name which is unlike, I call wrong, and in the case of names, false as well as wrong.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">654</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  That may be true, Socrates, in the case of pictures; they may be wrongly assigned; but not in the case of names—they must be always right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">655</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, what is the difference?</div><div class="sentence"> May I not go to a man and say to him, “This is your picture,” showing him his own likeness, or perhaps the likeness of a woman; and when I say “show,” I mean bring before the sense of sight.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">656</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">657</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And may I not go to him again, and say, “This is your name”?—for the name, like the picture, is an imitation.</div><div class="sentence"> May I not say to him—“This is your name”?</div><div class="sentence"> and may I not then bring to his sense of hearing the imitation of himself, when I say, “This is a man”; or of a female of the human species, when I say, “This is a woman,” as the case may be?</div><div class="sentence"> Is not all that quite possible?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">658</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I would fain agree with you, Socrates; and therefore I say, Granted.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">659</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  That is very good of you, if I am right, which need hardly be disputed at present.</div><div class="sentence"> But if I can assign names as well as pictures to objects, the right assignment of them we may call truth, and the wrong assignment of them falsehood.</div><div class="sentence"> Now if there be such a wrong assignment of names, there may also be a wrong or inappropriate assignment of verbs; and if of names and verbs then of the sentences, which are made up of them.</div><div class="sentence"> What do you say, Cratylus?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">660</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I agree; and think that what you say is very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">661</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And further, primitive nouns may be compared to pictures, and in pictures you may either give all the appropriate colours and figures, or you may not give them all—some may be wanting; or there may be too many or too much of them—may there not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">662</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">663</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And he who gives all gives a perfect picture or figure; and he who takes away or adds also gives a picture or figure, but not a good one.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">664</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">665</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In like manner, he who by syllables and letters imitates the nature of things, if he gives all that is appropriate will produce a good image, or in other words a name; but if he subtracts or perhaps adds a little, he will make an image but not a good one; whence I infer that some names are well and others ill made.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">666</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">667</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then the artist of names may be sometimes good, or he may be bad?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">668</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">669</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And this artist of names is called the legislator?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">670</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">671</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then like other artists the legislator may be good or he may be bad; it must surely be so if our former admissions hold good?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">672</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true, Socrates; but the case of language, you see, is different; for when by the help of grammar we assign the letters alpha or beta, or any other letters to a certain name, then, if we add, or subtract, or misplace a letter, the name which is written is not only written wrongly, but not written at all; and in any of these cases becomes other than a name.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">673</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But I doubt whether your view is altogether correct, Cratylus.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">674</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">675</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I believe that what you say may be true about numbers, which must be just what they are, or not be at all; for example, the number ten at once becomes other than ten if a unit be added or subtracted, and so of any other number:  but this does not apply to that which is qualitative or to anything which is represented under an image.</div><div class="sentence"> I should say rather that the image, if expressing in every point the entire reality, would no longer be an image.</div><div class="sentence"> Let us suppose the existence of two objects:  one of them shall be Cratylus, and the other the image of Cratylus; and we will suppose, further, that some God makes not only a representation such as a painter would make of your outward form and colour, but also creates an inward organization like yours, having the same warmth and softness; and into this infuses motion, and soul, and mind, such as you have, and in a word copies all your qualities, and places them by you in another form; would you say that this was Cratylus and the image of Cratylus, or that there were two Cratyluses?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">676</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that there were two Cratyluses.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">677</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then you see, my friend, that we must find some other principle of truth in images, and also in names; and not insist that an image is no longer an image when something is added or subtracted.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you not perceive that images are very far from having qualities which are the exact counterpart of the realities which they represent?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">678</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I see.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">679</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But then how ridiculous would be the effect of names on things, if they were exactly the same with them!</div><div class="sentence"> For they would be the doubles of them, and no one would be able to determine which were the names and which were the realities.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">680</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">681</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then fear not, but have the courage to admit that one name may be correctly and another incorrectly given; and do not insist that the name shall be exactly the same with the thing; but allow the occasional substitution of a wrong letter, and if of a letter also of a noun in a sentence, and if of a noun in a sentence also of a sentence which is not appropriate to the matter, and acknowledge that the thing may be named, and described, so long as the general character of the thing which you are describing is retained; and this, as you will remember, was remarked by Hermogenes and myself in the particular instance of the names of the letters.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">682</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I remember.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">683</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Good; and when the general character is preserved, even if some of the proper letters are wanting, still the thing is signified;—well, if all the letters are given; not well, when only a few of them are given.</div><div class="sentence"> I think that we had better admit this, lest we be punished like travellers in Aegina who wander about the street late at night:  and be likewise told by truth herself that we have arrived too late; or if not, you must find out some new notion of correctness of names, and no longer maintain that a name is the expression of a thing in letters or syllables; for if you say both, you will be inconsistent with yourself.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">684</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I quite acknowledge, Socrates, what you say to be very reasonable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">685</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then as we are agreed thus far, let us ask ourselves whether a name rightly imposed ought not to have the proper letters.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">686</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">687</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And the proper letters are those which are like the things?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">688</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">689</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Enough then of names which are rightly given.</div><div class="sentence"> And in names which are incorrectly given, the greater part may be supposed to be made up of proper and similar letters, or there would be no likeness; but there will be likewise a part which is improper and spoils the beauty and formation of the word:  you would admit that?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">690</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  There would be no use, Socrates, in my quarrelling with you, since I cannot be satisfied that a name which is incorrectly given is a name at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">691</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Do you admit a name to be the representation of a thing?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">692</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">693</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But do you not allow that some nouns are primitive, and some derived?</div></div>
