<div class="speech"><span class="ref">694</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, I do.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">695</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then if you admit that primitive or first nouns are representations of things, is there any better way of framing representations than by assimilating them to the objects as much as you can; or do you prefer the notion of Hermogenes and of many others, who say that names are conventional, and have a meaning to those who have agreed about them, and who have previous knowledge of the things intended by them, and that convention is the only principle; and whether you abide by our present convention, or make a new and opposite one, according to which you call small great and great small—that, they would say, makes no difference, if you are only agreed.</div><div class="sentence"> Which of these two notions do you prefer?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">696</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Representation by likeness, Socrates, is infinitely better than representation by any chance sign.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">697</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Very good:  but if the name is to be like the thing, the letters out of which the first names are composed must also be like things.</div><div class="sentence"> Returning to the image of the picture, I would ask, How could any one ever compose a picture which would be like anything at all, if there were not pigments in nature which resembled the things imitated, and out of which the picture is composed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">698</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Impossible.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">699</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  No more could names ever resemble any actually existing thing, unless the original elements of which they are compounded bore some degree of resemblance to the objects of which the names are the imitation:  And the original elements are letters?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">700</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">701</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let me now invite you to consider what Hermogenes and I were saying about sounds.</div><div class="sentence"> Do you agree with me that the letter rho is expressive of rapidity, motion, and hardness?</div><div class="sentence"> Were we right or wrong in saying so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">702</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that you were right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">703</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And that lamda was expressive of smoothness, and softness, and the like?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">704</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  There again you were right.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">705</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And yet, as you are aware, that which is called by us sklerotes, is by the Eretrians called skleroter.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">706</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">707</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But are the letters rho and sigma equivalents; and is there the same significance to them in the termination rho, which there is to us in sigma, or is there no significance to one of us?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">708</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Nay, surely there is a significance to both of us.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">709</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  In as far as they are like, or in as far as they are unlike?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">710</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  In as far as they are like.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">711</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Are they altogether alike?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">712</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; for the purpose of expressing motion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">713</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what do you say of the insertion of the lamda?</div><div class="sentence"> for that is expressive not of hardness but of softness.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">714</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Why, perhaps the letter lamda is wrongly inserted, Socrates, and should be altered into rho, as you were saying to Hermogenes and in my opinion rightly, when you spoke of adding and subtracting letters upon occasion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">715</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Good.</div><div class="sentence"> But still the word is intelligible to both of us; when I say skleros (hard), you know what I mean.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">716</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, my dear friend, and the explanation of that is custom.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">717</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And what is custom but convention?</div><div class="sentence"> I utter a sound which I understand, and you know that I understand the meaning of the sound:  this is what you are saying?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">718</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">719</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if when I speak you know my meaning, there is an indication given by me to you?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">720</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">721</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  This indication of my meaning may proceed from unlike as well as from like, for example in the lamda of sklerotes.</div><div class="sentence"> But if this is true, then you have made a convention with yourself, and the correctness of a name turns out to be convention, since letters which are unlike are indicative equally with those which are like, if they are sanctioned by custom and convention.</div><div class="sentence"> And even supposing that you distinguish custom from convention ever so much, still you must say that the signification of words is given by custom and not by likeness, for custom may indicate by the unlike as well as by the like.</div><div class="sentence"> But as we are agreed thus far, Cratylus (for I shall assume that your silence gives consent), then custom and convention must be supposed to contribute to the indication of our thoughts; for suppose we take the instance of number, how can you ever imagine, my good friend, that you will find names resembling every individual number, unless you allow that which you term convention and agreement to have authority in determining the correctness of names?</div><div class="sentence"> I quite agree with you that words should as far as possible resemble things; but I fear that this dragging in of resemblance, as Hermogenes says, is a shabby thing, which has to be supplemented by the mechanical aid of convention with a view to correctness; for I believe that if we could always, or almost always, use likenesses, which are perfectly appropriate, this would be the most perfect state of language; as the opposite is the most imperfect.</div><div class="sentence"> But let me ask you, what is the force of names, and what is the use of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">722</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  The use of names, Socrates, as I should imagine, is to inform:  the simple truth is, that he who knows names knows also the things which are expressed by them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">723</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  I suppose you mean to say, Cratylus, that as the name is, so also is the thing; and that he who knows the one will also know the other, because they are similars, and all similars fall under the same art or science; and therefore you would say that he who knows names will also know things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">724</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  That is precisely what I mean.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">725</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But let us consider what is the nature of this information about things which, according to you, is given us by names.</div><div class="sentence"> Is it the best sort of information?</div><div class="sentence"> or is there any other?</div><div class="sentence"> What do you say?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">726</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I believe that to be both the only and the best sort of information about them; there can be no other.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">727</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But do you believe that in the discovery of them, he who discovers the names discovers also the things; or is this only the method of instruction, and is there some other method of enquiry and discovery.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">728</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I certainly believe that the methods of enquiry and discovery are of the same nature as instruction.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">729</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but do you not see, Cratylus, that he who follows names in the search after things, and analyses their meaning, is in great danger of being deceived?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">730</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  How so?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">731</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why clearly he who first gave names gave them according to his conception of the things which they signified—did he not?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">732</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">733</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And if his conception was erroneous, and he gave names according to his conception, in what position shall we who are his followers find ourselves?</div><div class="sentence"> Shall we not be deceived by him?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">734</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  But, Socrates, am I not right in thinking that he must surely have known; or else, as I was saying, his names would not be names at all?</div><div class="sentence"> And you have a clear proof that he has not missed the truth, and the proof is—that he is perfectly consistent.</div><div class="sentence"> Did you ever observe in speaking that all the words which you utter have a common character and purpose?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">735</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But that, friend Cratylus, is no answer.</div><div class="sentence"> For if he did begin in error, he may have forced the remainder into agreement with the original error and with himself; there would be nothing strange in this, any more than in geometrical diagrams, which have often a slight and invisible flaw in the first part of the process, and are consistently mistaken in the long deductions which follow.</div><div class="sentence"> And this is the reason why every man should expend his chief thought and attention on the consideration of his first principles: —are they or are they not rightly laid down?</div><div class="sentence"> and when he has duly sifted them, all the rest will follow.</div><div class="sentence"> Now I should be astonished to find that names are really consistent.</div><div class="sentence"> And here let us revert to our former discussion:  Were we not saying that all things are in motion and progress and flux, and that this idea of motion is expressed by names?</div><div class="sentence"> Do you not conceive that to be the meaning of them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">736</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes; that is assuredly their meaning, and the true meaning.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">737</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us revert to episteme (knowledge) and observe how ambiguous this word is, seeming rather to signify stopping the soul at things than going round with them; and therefore we should leave the beginning as at present, and not reject the epsilon, but make an insertion of an iota instead of an epsilon (not pioteme, but epiisteme).</div><div class="sentence"> Take another example:  bebaion (sure) is clearly the expression of station and position, and not of motion.</div><div class="sentence"> Again, the word istoria (enquiry) bears upon the face of it the stopping (istanai) of the stream; and the word piston (faithful) certainly indicates cessation of motion; then, again, mneme (memory), as any one may see, expresses rest in the soul, and not motion.</div><div class="sentence"> Moreover, words such as amartia and sumphora, which have a bad sense, viewed in the light of their etymologies will be the same as sunesis and episteme and other words which have a good sense (compare omartein, sunienai, epesthai, sumpheresthai); and much the same may be said of amathia and akolasia, for amathia may be explained as e ama theo iontos poreia, and akolasia as e akolouthia tois pragmasin.</div><div class="sentence"> Thus the names which in these instances we find to have the worst sense, will turn out to be framed on the same principle as those which have the best.</div><div class="sentence"> And any one I believe who would take the trouble might find many other examples in which the giver of names indicates, not that things are in motion or progress, but that they are at rest; which is the opposite of motion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">738</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes, Socrates, but observe; the greater number express motion.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">739</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  What of that, Cratylus?</div><div class="sentence"> Are we to count them like votes?</div><div class="sentence"> and is correctness of names the voice of the majority?</div><div class="sentence"> Are we to say of whichever sort there are most, those are the true ones?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">740</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  No; that is not reasonable.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">741</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly not.</div><div class="sentence"> But let us have done with this question and proceed to another, about which I should like to know whether you think with me.</div><div class="sentence"> Were we not lately acknowledging that the first givers of names in states, both Hellenic and barbarous, were the legislators, and that the art which gave names was the art of the legislator?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">742</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Quite true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">743</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Tell me, then, did the first legislators, who were the givers of the first names, know or not know the things which they named?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">744</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  They must have known, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">745</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Why, yes, friend Cratylus, they could hardly have been ignorant.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">746</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say not.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">747</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us return to the point from which we digressed.</div><div class="sentence"> You were saying, if you remember, that he who gave names must have known the things which he named; are you still of that opinion?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">748</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I am.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">749</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And would you say that the giver of the first names had also a knowledge of the things which he named?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">750</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">751</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But how could he have learned or discovered things from names if the primitive names were not yet given?</div><div class="sentence"> For, if we are correct in our view, the only way of learning and discovering things, is either to discover names for ourselves or to learn them from others.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">752</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I think that there is a good deal in what you say, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">753</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if things are only to be known through names, how can we suppose that the givers of names had knowledge, or were legislators before there were names at all, and therefore before they could have known them?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">754</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I believe, Socrates, the true account of the matter to be, that a power more than human gave things their first names, and that the names which are thus given are necessarily their true names.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">755</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then how came the giver of the names, if he was an inspired being or God, to contradict himself?</div><div class="sentence"> For were we not saying just now that he made some names expressive of rest and others of motion?</div><div class="sentence"> Were we mistaken?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">756</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  But I suppose one of the two not to be names at all.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">757</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And which, then, did he make, my good friend; those which are expressive of rest, or those which are expressive of motion?</div><div class="sentence"> This is a point which, as I said before, cannot be determined by counting them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">758</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  No; not in that way, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">759</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if this is a battle of names, some of them asserting that they are like the truth, others contending that THEY are, how or by what criterion are we to decide between them?</div><div class="sentence"> For there are no other names to which appeal can be made, but obviously recourse must be had to another standard which, without employing names, will make clear which of the two are right; and this must be a standard which shows the truth of things.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">760</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I agree.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">761</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But if that is true, Cratylus, then I suppose that things may be known without names?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">762</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">763</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  But how would you expect to know them?</div><div class="sentence"> What other way can there be of knowing them, except the true and natural way, through their affinities, when they are akin to each other, and through themselves?</div><div class="sentence"> For that which is other and different from them must signify something other and different from them.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">764</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  What you are saying is, I think, true.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">765</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Well, but reflect; have we not several times acknowledged that names rightly given are the likenesses and images of the things which they name?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">766</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Yes.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">767</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Let us suppose that to any extent you please you can learn things through the medium of names, and suppose also that you can learn them from the things themselves—which is likely to be the nobler and clearer way; to learn of the image, whether the image and the truth of which the image is the expression have been rightly conceived, or to learn of the truth whether the truth and the image of it have been duly executed?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">768</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I should say that we must learn of the truth.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">769</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  How real existence is to be studied or discovered is, I suspect, beyond you and me.</div><div class="sentence"> But we may admit so much, that the knowledge of things is not to be derived from names.</div><div class="sentence"> No; they must be studied and investigated in themselves.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">770</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Clearly, Socrates.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">771</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  There is another point.</div><div class="sentence"> I should not like us to be imposed upon by the appearance of such a multitude of names, all tending in the same direction.</div><div class="sentence"> I myself do not deny that the givers of names did really give them under the idea that all things were in motion and flux; which was their sincere but, I think, mistaken opinion.</div><div class="sentence"> And having fallen into a kind of whirlpool themselves, they are carried round, and want to drag us in after them.</div><div class="sentence"> There is a matter, master Cratylus, about which I often dream, and should like to ask your opinion:  Tell me, whether there is or is not any absolute beauty or good, or any other absolute existence?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">772</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly, Socrates, I think so.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">773</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then let us seek the true beauty:  not asking whether a face is fair, or anything of that sort, for all such things appear to be in a flux; but let us ask whether the true beauty is not always beautiful.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">774</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">775</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  And can we rightly speak of a beauty which is always passing away, and is first this and then that; must not the same thing be born and retire and vanish while the word is in our mouths?</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">776</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Undoubtedly.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">777</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then how can that be a real thing which is never in the same state?</div><div class="sentence"> for obviously things which are the same cannot change while they remain the same; and if they are always the same and in the same state, and never depart from their original form, they can never change or be moved.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">778</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Certainly they cannot.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">779</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nor yet can they be known by any one; for at the moment that the observer approaches, then they become other and of another nature, so that you cannot get any further in knowing their nature or state, for you cannot know that which has no state.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">780</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  True.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">781</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Nor can we reasonably say, Cratylus, that there is knowledge at all, if everything is in a state of transition and there is nothing abiding; for knowledge too cannot continue to be knowledge unless continuing always to abide and exist.</div><div class="sentence"> But if the very nature of knowledge changes, at the time when the change occurs there will be no knowledge; and if the transition is always going on, there will always be no knowledge, and, according to this view, there will be no one to know and nothing to be known:  but if that which knows and that which is known exists ever, and the beautiful and the good and every other thing also exist, then I do not think that they can resemble a process or flux, as we were just now supposing.</div><div class="sentence"> Whether there is this eternal nature in things, or whether the truth is what Heracleitus and his followers and many others say, is a question hard to determine; and no man of sense will like to put himself or the education of his mind in the power of names:  neither will he so far trust names or the givers of names as to be confident in any knowledge which condemns himself and other existences to an unhealthy state of unreality; he will not believe that all things leak like a pot, or imagine that the world is a man who has a running at the nose.</div><div class="sentence"> This may be true, Cratylus, but is also very likely to be untrue; and therefore I would not have you be too easily persuaded of it.</div><div class="sentence"> Reflect well and like a man, and do not easily accept such a doctrine; for you are young and of an age to learn.</div><div class="sentence"> And when you have found the truth, come and tell me.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">782</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  I will do as you say, though I can assure you, Socrates, that I have been considering the matter already, and the result of a great deal of trouble and consideration is that I incline to Heracleitus.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">783</span><div class="speaker">SOCRATES</div><div class="sentence">  Then, another day, my friend, when you come back, you shall give me a lesson; but at present, go into the country, as you are intending, and Hermogenes shall set you on your way.</div></div>
<div class="speech"><span class="ref">784</span><div class="speaker">CRATYLUS</div><div class="sentence">  Very good, Socrates; I hope, however, that you will continue to think about these things yourself.</div></div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
